Watkins's *HERO SLAY SERPENT* formula treats Illuyanka as another reflex of the inherited IE formula, and Aži Dahāka and Illuyanka therefore stand alongside one another within Watkins's wider Indo-European-and-adjacent comparative repertory.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V on the Iranian and Hurro-Hittite reflexes within the IE *HERO SLAY SERPENT* formula; per chaoskampf scope.md on Ch6 as the Illuyanka chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V chs. 44-48

=== Reception (briefly)

The most influential post-Avestan-and-Pahlavi reception of the Aži Dahāka tradition is the New Persian Shāhnāmeh of Firdowsi, composed c. 1000 CE, which recasts Thraētaona as Fereydun and recasts Aži Dahāka as the tyrant Zahhāk in an Islamic-era epic-literary form. The chapter flags this reception's existence but does not narrate its content, in line with the brief's stipulation that the Shāhnāmeh material is out of primary scope.footnote:[Per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml on the Shāhnāmeh as Islamic-era reception out of primary scope; Watkins 1995, Part V ch. 46, on the Shāhnāmeh as the principal post-Pahlavi reception; per chaoskampf scope.md on the Period exclusion of post-medieval primary material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V ch. 46

The Shāhnāmeh's recasting of the tradition operates at the level of name-reformations (Thraētaona → Frēdōn → Fereydun; Aži Dahāka → Dahāg → Zahhāk), at the level of narrative-amplification (the dragon-tyrant is given a distinctive serpent-shoulders motif in the Shāhnāmeh that is a Firdowsi-period elaboration), and at the level of literary register (the New Persian *masnavi* verse-form rather than the Pahlavi prose-compilation of the *Bundahišn*).footnote:[Per chaoskampf glossary.yaml on the onomastic chains; per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml on the Shāhnāmeh recasting at multiple levels.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=passim

Because the Shāhnāmeh's elaborations are post-medieval Islamic-era literary productions, and because the brief stipulates that the chapter flags rather than narrates the reception, the chapter does not treat the Zahhāk-of-Firdowsi material as a primary witness to the Iranian tradition. It is a reception-witness that the present book reserves for its concluding reception coda rather than admitting into the Part I story-chapter on the Avestan-Pahlavi material.footnote:[Per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml on the chapter's flagging-rather-than-narrating treatment; per chaoskampf scope.md on the reception coda for post-medieval material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V ch. 46

The chapter's discipline on the Shāhnāmeh boundary is therefore that the New Persian Fereydun-Zahhāk material is named only where its existence is structurally relevant to the Avestan-Pahlavi material's reception-history, that the Avestan-Pahlavi diacritical forms (Thraētaona and Aži Dahāka) are used throughout the body, and that the New Persian forms (Fereydun and Zahhāk) appear only in this brief reception note.footnote:[Per chaoskampf glossary.yaml on the Avestan-Pahlavi-vs-New-Persian onomastic discipline; per brief 13-thraetaona-azidahaka.yaml on the Shāhnāmeh boundary.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=passim

The Shāhnāmeh reception is one of several long-trajectory reception threads that the Avestan-Pahlavi Aži Dahāka material has carried into later Iranian-and-related cultural spheres. The dragon-tyrant motif has further reflexes in Armenian, Caucasian, and Central Asian traditions (Russell 1987 on the Armenian reception is the chaoskampf scope.md's Vahagn / Vishaps anchor), and the chapter notes the existence of these threads as a forward-link to the Vahagn-and-Vishaps family-chapter elsewhere in the book.footnote:[Per chaoskampf scope.md on the Vahagn / Vishaps family-chapter as a related-reception locus; Watkins 1995, Part V ch. 46, on the long-trajectory reception of the Aži Dahāka material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V ch. 46

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Vedic — Trita Āptya (RV 10.99.6) and Indra-Vṛtra (Ch14, Part II C5)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Hebrew Bible / Christian — Isaiah 27:1 binding-and-final-slaying eschatology and Revelation 20 binding-of-Satan (long-discussed Achaemenid-Hellenistic cross-influence question; Forsyth 1987)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Norse — Jörmungandr at Ragnarök as eschatological pendant (Part II C4)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Hurro-Hittite — Illuyanka structural parallel via Watkins's IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (Part II C5)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Reception — Shāhnāmeh / Fereydun-Zahhāk (Firdowsi c. 1000 CE; out of primary scope per brief; Part II reception coda)
```

---

# Input file: chapters/06-illuyanka.adoc

```
[[ch-06-illuyanka]]
== Tarḫunna vs. Illuyanka: The Purulli Festival

=== Source overview

The myth of Tarḫunna and Illuyanka survives on cuneiform tablets recovered from the Hittite royal archives at the capital Hattusa, modern Boğazköy in central Anatolia.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Beckman 1982, "The Anatolian Myth of Illuyanka," JANES 14: 11–25, on the manuscript provenance.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The text is catalogued in Emmanuel Laroche's *Catalogue des textes hittites* as CTH 321, the dossier number under which the surviving manuscripts of the Illuyanka myth are grouped.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka," giving the CTH 321 designation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The composition is preserved on multiple manuscripts of varying completeness rather than on a single tablet, and modern editions are composite reconstructions from these witnesses.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka," on the manuscript group; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the witness situation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=beckman-1982-illuyanka ; loc=JANES 14: 11-25
The language of CTH 321 is Hittite, the Indo-European language of the Hittite royal court used for the bulk of the cuneiform literary corpus from Hattusa.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, general introduction to *Hittite Myths* on the linguistic situation of the corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=general introduction

The CTH 321 dossier preserves two distinct narrative accounts of the same combat between the storm-god and the serpent Illuyanka, conventionally numbered Version 1 and Version 2 by modern editors.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2, the two versions; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the two-version structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2
Both versions are written by the same scribal tradition on the same tablet group and are presented side by side rather than as a single composite narrative.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the side-by-side presentation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
Modern scholarship treats the two versions as co-equal scribal accounts rather than ranking one as primary, since neither the scribes themselves nor the surviving cult contexts privilege one over the other.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction

The framing material on the tablets identifies the composition as a recitation associated with the _purulli_ festival, the Hittite spring liturgical occasion at which the myth was performed.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, opening colophon naming the purulli festival; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1 opening
The named scribal author in the colophon is Kella, identified on the tablet as the priest who set down the recitation of the _purulli_ festival.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, colophon naming Kella; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1 colophon

The editio princeps used as the philological anchor for this chapter is Gary Beckman's "The Anatolian Myth of Illuyanka," published in the *Journal of the Ancient Near Eastern Society* 14 (1982): 11–25.footnote:[Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, full article.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=beckman-1982-illuyanka ; loc=JANES 14: 11-25
The working English translation cited in this chapter is Harry A. Hoffner Jr.'s rendering in *Hittite Myths*, 2nd edition (Society of Biblical Literature, 1998), where the two versions are printed as §1 and §2 of the "Illuyanka" entry.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka," §§1–2.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2
The Hoffner edition presents the two versions in continuous English translation with section numbering that follows the tablet-paragraphs of the underlying Hittite text.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka," editorial conventions and §§1–2.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka editorial conventions

Calvert Watkins's *How to Kill a Dragon* (Oxford, 1995) provides the principal Indo-European linguistic and poetic-formula analysis of the Hittite text, treating Illuyanka as one of the four main demonstration cases for the reconstructed combat formula.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46, on Illuyanka and the Indo-European combat formula in Hittite.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46
M.L. West's *The East Face of Helicon* (Oxford, 1997) treats the Hittite dragon-combat in the broader context of Indo-European and Near Eastern transmission, with cross-references to Greek and Levantine cognates.footnote:[West 1997, references on the Hittite dragon-combat in IE and Near-Eastern context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1997 ; loc=references on the Hittite dragon-combat
Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy* (Princeton, 1987) places the Illuyanka combat within the Anatolian segment of his long history of the combat-myth from Sumer through Christian apocalyptic.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, ch. 3 on Anatolian combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=ch. 3

Two features of this dossier set it apart from most of the combat-myths gathered in this book. The first is the on-tablet ritual colophon that names the festival to which the recitation belongs. The second is the side-by-side preservation of two versions whose scribes did not feel obliged to choose between them. The chapter is built around those two features.

=== Setting and context — the storm-god of Nerik

Tarḫunna is the Hittite storm-god, the head of the Hittite state pantheon during the Old and New Kingdom periods of the second millennium BCE.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, general introduction on Hittite religion and the storm-god.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=general introduction
The Hurrian-language counterpart of Tarḫunna is Teshub, and the same deity appears under the Hurrian name in the Kumarbi Cycle and other Hurrian-derived compositions of the Hittite archive.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, general introduction on Hittite-Hurrian theological correspondences.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=general introduction
The Illuyanka myth is a Hittite-language composition and consequently uses Tarḫunna throughout, distinguishing it from the Hurrian-derived material that uses Teshub.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2, throughout.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2

Nerik is a northern Hittite cult-centre and the principal locus of the storm-god's cult, and is the site at which the _purulli_ festival was annually celebrated.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka" on Nerik and the purulli festival.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
Nerik lay in the northern frontier zone of the Hittite kingdom and was for long stretches of the second millennium under the control of the Kaska peoples, with the consequence that the cult of the storm-god of Nerik was at times conducted in exile from the city.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka" on Nerik's geopolitical situation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The cult of the storm-god of Nerik is tied into Hittite royal religion, and the _purulli_ festival therefore connects the Illuyanka recitation to the legitimation of the Hittite kingship.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka" on the royal-religious context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction

The _purulli_ festival is a spring liturgical occasion, and the surviving framing texts identify its purpose as ensuring the prosperity of the land and the cosmic order in the agricultural year ahead.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, opening; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the purulli framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1 opening
The term _purulli_ is preserved with a determinative for festival in the cuneiform script and is a Hittite ritual term rather than a loanword from Hurrian or Akkadian.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, glossary entry under purulli; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the term's status.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=glossary entry purulli

The opening framing of CTH 321 consists of a scribal colophon that explicitly states that what follows is the recitation associated with the _purulli_ festival of the storm-god.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, opening colophon; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1 opening colophon
The framing colophon links the festival's annual recurrence to the recitation of the combat narrative, embedding the myth in the liturgical year as the verbal component of the festival.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1 framing; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1 framing
This explicit ritual embedding is what marks the Illuyanka myth as one of the few ancient Near Eastern combat myths whose liturgical context is directly attested on the tablet itself rather than reconstructed from later cult evidence.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the unusually explicit framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction

Inara is the daughter of the storm-god Tarḫunna and is the principal divine actor of Version 1 of the myth.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on Inara as Tarḫunna's daughter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Hupasiya is a mortal man, the human protagonist of Version 1, whose home town is named in the text as Ziggaratta.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on Hupasiya and his town Ziggaratta.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Illuyanka is a serpent or dragon, the cosmic adversary of the storm-god, and the antagonist whose defeat in either version constitutes the combat that the festival commemorates.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2, on Illuyanka as serpent-adversary.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2
The Hittite noun *illuyanka-* is itself the common-noun word for "snake" or "serpent," used as a proper name for the cosmic adversary in CTH 321.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46, on the etymology of Illuyanka as the common-noun "serpent"; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46

Forsyth situates the Illuyanka combat within the family of Anatolian combat-myths that includes the Kumarbi Cycle's Ullikummi and Hedammu, distinguishing the Hittite-language Illuyanka from the Hurrian-derived material in register and ritual function.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, ch. 3 on Anatolian combat-myths.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=ch. 3

=== Version 1 — Inara and Hupasiya

Version 1 of the Illuyanka myth, preserved as Hoffner's "Illuyanka" §1, opens with the storm-god and the serpent Illuyanka meeting in combat at a place called Kiskilussa.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, opening narrative at Kiskilussa.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
In this first encounter, Illuyanka defeats Tarḫunna: the serpent overcomes the storm-god, an outcome that establishes the narrative problem the rest of Version 1 addresses.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on the storm-god's initial defeat by Illuyanka.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Defeated, Tarḫunna calls upon all the gods for help, and Inara, his daughter, comes forward and devises a stratagem to enable a second engagement.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on the storm-god's appeal and Inara's response.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1

Inara prepares a great banquet, with vessels filled with all kinds of drink — wine, *marnuwan*-beer, and *walhi*-beer are named — designed to lure Illuyanka out of its hole.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on the banquet preparation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Inara then descends to the town of Ziggaratta and there finds Hupasiya, a mortal man, whose recruitment to her plan is the next step.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on Inara's visit to Ziggaratta and Hupasiya.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Inara proposes a bargain to Hupasiya: he is to assist her against the serpent, and in exchange she offers to spend a night with him.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on Inara's bargain with Hupasiya.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Hupasiya agrees to the bargain, and the text records the night spent between them as a fulfilled stipulation of the agreement.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on the consummated bargain.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1

Inara conducts Hupasiya to the site of the planned banquet, and there conceals him so that he is positioned to act once the serpent has come out of its hole.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on the concealment of Hupasiya at the banquet site.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Inara dresses herself in finery and summons Illuyanka and his offspring out of their hole to the banquet.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on Inara's summoning of the serpent.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Illuyanka and his offspring come up from the hole and eat and drink at the banquet that Inara has prepared.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on the serpent's feast.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
The serpents drink to satiation, and the text states that they are no longer able to descend back into their hole because they are too engorged.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on the serpents' inability to return to the hole.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1

While the serpents are immobilised by their gorging, Hupasiya emerges from concealment and binds Illuyanka with a rope.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on Hupasiya's binding of Illuyanka.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
The storm-god Tarḫunna then returns, accompanied by the assembled gods, and slays the bound Illuyanka.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on the storm-god's killing of the bound Illuyanka.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
The storm-god's killing of the serpent in Version 1 is achieved only because Illuyanka has been immobilised by Hupasiya's binding, not by direct combat — the divine victory depends on the mortal helper.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1; Forsyth 1987, ch. 3 on the mortal helper's role.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1

After the killing, Inara takes Hupasiya to a house she has built for him on a cliff in the land of Tarukka, and there installs him.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on the house in the land of Tarukka.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Inara imposes a prohibition on Hupasiya: he is forbidden from looking out of the window of his cliffside house, lest he see his wife and children whom he has left behind.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on Inara's prohibition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
After twenty days, Hupasiya looks out of the window in defiance of the prohibition and sees his wife and children.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on Hupasiya's transgression.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1
Upon Inara's return, Hupasiya begs to be sent home to his family, and Inara reacts in anger to his transgression of her prohibition.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, on Hupasiya's request and Inara's reaction.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1

The text at this point becomes broken, and the precise fate of Hupasiya in Version 1 is not preserved in continuous form on the surviving manuscripts.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, marked as broken at the close of the Hupasiya episode.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1 (broken)

[LACUNA: the closing section of Version 1, including the precise fate of Hupasiya and the framing of the purulli festival as the myth's aetiology, is partially broken on the surviving manuscripts | source: Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1, end of tablet | scholarly_reconstruction: the ritual colophon connecting the narrative to the purulli festival is extant in the framing material, but the narrative continuity between Hupasiya's transgression and the festival aetiology cannot be fully reconstructed from the surviving witnesses.]

The framing material that survives at the close of Version 1 connects the recitation back to the _purulli_ festival, identifying the narrative just told as the ritual aetiology of the spring observance.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1 closing framing; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1 closing framing
The closing of Version 1 also includes liturgical material referring to a building or house in which the gods convene, and to a procession of cult objects as part of the festival.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1 closing; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the ritual closing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1 closing

Version 1 is thus structured as a god–goddess–mortal-man triad, with the storm-god as the threatened sovereign, Inara as the divine strategist, and Hupasiya as the indispensable mortal binder.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §1; Forsyth 1987, ch. 3, on the triadic structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §1

=== Version 2 — the storm-god's son and the bride-price

Version 2 of the Illuyanka myth, preserved as Hoffner's "Illuyanka" §2, opens with a different combat scenario: the serpent Illuyanka defeats the storm-god in a manner that involves bodily mutilation rather than mere overpowering.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, opening combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2
In Version 2, Illuyanka takes from the storm-god his heart and his eyes, removing these organs as the prize of his victory.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the loss of heart and eyes.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2

The mutilated storm-god, now without heart and eyes, takes a daughter of a poor man as his wife, with whom he begets a son.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the storm-god's mortal marriage and son.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2
The storm-god's son grows to maturity within his mortal mother's family without the text recording the kind of supernatural acceleration that characterises some other Near Eastern hero-births.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the son's upbringing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2
The son is then given in marriage into the household of Illuyanka, taking as his wife a daughter of the serpent.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the marriage into Illuyanka's house.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2

Before the son enters Illuyanka's household, the storm-god instructs him: when his bride-price is to be set, he is to ask for the storm-god's heart and eyes — the organs Illuyanka had taken — as the gift demanded from the bride's family.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the storm-god's instruction concerning the bride-price.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2
The son enters Illuyanka's house and demands as bride-price the storm-god's heart and eyes.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the son's demand.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2
Illuyanka's household yields the heart and the eyes to the son, who carries them back to his father the storm-god.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the recovery of the organs.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2
The storm-god, with his heart and eyes restored, regains his full divine vigour and prepares for a renewed combat with Illuyanka.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the restoration of the storm-god.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2

[LACUNA: the mid-narrative transitions of Version 2, between the son's marriage into Illuyanka's house and the recovery of the organs, are broken on the surviving manuscripts | source: Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, mid-narrative | scholarly_reconstruction: the tablet preserves enough text for the basic narrative arc to be followed, but the specific staging of the bride-price negotiation and any intervening episodes are not continuously preserved.]

The storm-god then engages Illuyanka anew, this time in a combat at the sea, and slays the serpent.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the renewed combat at the sea.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2
In the renewed combat, the storm-god's son must also be killed, because by virtue of his marriage into Illuyanka's house he has become bound to the serpent's family by the obligations of kinship.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the killing of the son alongside Illuyanka.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2
The son himself acknowledges the obligation, and according to the surviving text he calls upon his father the storm-god to kill him along with Illuyanka, since he has kept faith with his father-in-law's house and cannot be spared.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the son's request for his own death.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2
The storm-god kills both Illuyanka and his own son in the same combat, and the text registers no exemption for the son despite his role in the recovery of the organs.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2, on the dual killing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2

The closing of Version 2, like that of Version 1, returns to the framing of the _purulli_ festival, identifying the recitation as the liturgical companion of the spring rite at Nerik.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2 closing framing; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2 closing
Version 2 is thus structured around a loss-and-recovery pattern: the storm-god's organs are taken, are restored through the agency of his half-mortal son, and the second combat completes what the first could not.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §2; Forsyth 1987, ch. 3, on the loss-and-recovery pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §2

=== Hinge — what the two versions share, where they diverge

The two versions share a common backbone: an initial defeat of the storm-god by Illuyanka, the recruitment of an indirect agent (a mortal man in Version 1, a half-mortal son in Version 2), and a second engagement in which the serpent is killed.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the shared narrative armature.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The two versions diverge sharply on the role of the mortal collaborator: in Version 1 the mortal Hupasiya is rewarded and then punished for transgression; in Version 2 the storm-god's son is killed for keeping faith with the wrong household.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2; Forsyth 1987, ch. 3.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2
The two versions also diverge on the mode of the storm-god's initial defeat: Version 1 narrates an unspecified overpowering, while Version 2 specifies bodily mutilation through the loss of heart and eyes.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2

Beckman in the editio princeps argues that the two versions are best understood as alternative liturgical recitations of the same myth, drawn from different cult centres or different ritual occasions and gathered onto the same tablet group by the scribes of Hattusa.footnote:[Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the relation between the two versions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=beckman-1982-illuyanka ; loc=JANES 14: 11-25
Hoffner notes that the question of whether one version is older than the other has been debated, but that the surviving tablet group does not itself establish a relative chronology between them.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka," on the chronological question.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction

=== The ritual context — purulli at Nerik

The _purulli_ festival was an annual spring observance of the Hittite cultic year, performed at Nerik when the cult could be conducted there and at substitute locations (notably Hakmis) when Nerik was not under Hittite control.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka" on the festival's annual cycle and locations.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The festival is referenced in Hittite ritual texts beyond CTH 321 itself, and these ritual descriptions establish its character as a multi-day cultic event involving the storm-god of Nerik.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka" on the festival's broader cultic attestation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The framing colophons of CTH 321 indicate that the recitation of the Illuyanka myth was a verbal component of the festival, performed alongside the cultic actions described in the ritual texts.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2 framing material; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the recitation's role in the festival.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka framing material

The combat narrative is thus presented as the verbal score of an annual ritual: the killing of the serpent is re-enacted each spring through recitation, and the festival's recurrence is the festival's whole point.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the cyclical character of the rite.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The cyclical-maintenance character of the Illuyanka myth — combat as something annually re-enacted rather than once-and-done — is one of the principal grounds on which modern scholarship pairs the Hittite material with the Egyptian Ra-Apep complex as the type-cases of the cyclical-maintenance subtype.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction

The cult of the storm-god of Nerik is closely tied to the Hittite kingship: the king himself participated in the _purulli_ festival, and royal documents register the storm-god of Nerik among the principal patron deities of the Hittite state.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka" on the royal-religious dimension.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The connection between the _purulli_ festival and Hittite kingship gives the Illuyanka myth a double function: ritual-aetiological for the spring festival and royal-legitimating for the dynasty under whose authority the festival was performed.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Forsyth 1987, ch. 3 on the royal dimension.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The historical period over which the _purulli_ festival is attested spans the Old Hittite through the New Hittite kingdom, with the bulk of the surviving tablets dating to the New Hittite period of the late second millennium BCE.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka" on the chronological span of the attestation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction

[INFERENCE: the recitation of both Version 1 and Version 2 in connection with the same festival suggests that the Hittite cult tolerated, and perhaps required, narrative redundancy — the same combat told in two manners as part of a single liturgical occasion | basis: Hoffner 1998 introduction notes that both versions sit on the same tablet group framed by purulli colophons, and Beckman 1982 on the side-by-side scribal presentation; the inference draws together those observations | risk: the surviving tablets do not state explicitly that both versions were recited in the same festival, only that both are connected to the festival in their framing.]

The Illuyanka myth's explicit ritual embedding distinguishes it from the cosmogonic combat-myths of Mesopotamia (Enūma Eliš, Lugal-e), where the ritual context must be reconstructed from secondary evidence, and from the Levantine Baal Cycle, where the connection to specific festivals is debated.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, ch. 3 on the contrast with Mesopotamian and Levantine combat-myths; Hoffner 1998, introduction.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=ch. 3
The myth's framing as the verbal counterpart of an annual rite places it in the cyclical-maintenance subtype of combat-myth — combat re-enacted to preserve an order that is always under threat, rather than combat-as-cosmogony or combat-as-once-only-sovereignty-establishment.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Forsyth 1987, ch. 3.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The fact that two versions coexist on the same tablet group also distinguishes the Illuyanka dossier from many other ancient Near Eastern combat-myths, where a single canonical version is sought and variant readings are treated as scribal errors rather than as parallel recitations.footnote:[Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25, on the unusual co-equal scribal handling; Hoffner 1998, introduction.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=beckman-1982-illuyanka ; loc=JANES 14: 11-25

=== The Watkins formula

Calvert Watkins, in *How to Kill a Dragon* (1995), identifies a reconstructed Indo-European poetic formula of the form [.smallcaps]#hero slay serpent#, in which a subject-noun, a verb of killing, and a serpent-object together name the combat.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 and the volume's framing chapters on the HERO SLAY SERPENT formula.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46
The formula is anchored in Watkins's analysis on the Vedic phrase _áhann áhim_, "he slew the serpent," from Rigveda 1.32.1, which he treats as the reflex closest to the reconstructed Indo-European form.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 on _áhann áhim_ as the reflex anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46
Watkins argues that the Hittite text of CTH 321 preserves a cognate formulation in the verb-and-object pairing *kuenta… illuyanka-* — "he slew… the serpent" — which uses a verb of killing etymologically related to the Vedic *han-* and an object that is the common-noun "serpent."footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 on the Hittite cognate formulation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46

The Hittite reflex thus places Illuyanka, alongside Vedic Vṛtra and Avestan Aži Dahāka, among the principal demonstration cases for Watkins's reconstructed Indo-European combat-myth formula.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 on Illuyanka as a demonstration case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46
Watkins's argument turns on lexical and syntactic correspondences across the Indo-European daughter languages, not on narrative-structural parallels alone, and is therefore methodologically distinct from a Frazerian or Dumézilian comparativism.footnote:[Watkins 1995, methodological framing chapters on the lexical-syntactic basis of the reconstruction.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=methodological framing chapters
The presence of the formula in Hittite is significant for the dating of the reconstructed formula, since Hittite is the earliest-attested Indo-European language and an early-branching member of the family.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 and methodological framing on the chronological significance of the Hittite reflex.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46

West reinforces Watkins's reading by placing the Hittite material within the broader Near Eastern transmission picture, noting that Illuyanka stands at the intersection of Indo-European inheritance and Anatolian local cult.footnote:[West 1997, references on the Hittite dragon-combat as an Indo-European / Near-Eastern intersection.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-1997 ; loc=Hittite dragon-combat references
The full elaboration of the [.smallcaps]#hero slay serpent# formula, including the Greek (Apollo, Heracles), Germanic (Sigurd, Beowulf), and Iranian (Thraētaona vs. Aži Dahāka) reflexes, is reserved for the comparative chapter on Indo-European combat-myth (Part II C5), and is not developed further in the present chapter.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46 and following chapters; cross-reference to Part II C5 of this volume.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46 and following chapters

=== Closing — the Illuyanka dossier and its cyclical work

The Illuyanka dossier consists of two co-equal scribal versions of a single combat between Tarḫunna and Illuyanka, framed in their tablet form as the recitation belonging to the _purulli_ festival of the storm-god of Nerik.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, "Illuyanka" §§1–2 with framing colophons; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka §§1-2
The dossier's distinctive contribution to the comparative study of the combat-myth is its explicit, on-tablet ritual embedding: the combat is presented as the verbal score of an annual rite, not as a one-off cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing act.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Beckman 1982, JANES 14: 11–25; Forsyth 1987, ch. 3.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction

This cyclical-maintenance framing pairs the Illuyanka myth with the Egyptian Ra-Apep complex as the two type-cases of combat-myth-as-annual-recurrence within the ancient Near Eastern corpus.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Forsyth 1987, ch. 3 on the cyclical pairing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction
The Illuyanka myth's place in the Indo-European combat-myth complex, secured by Watkins's formula analysis, gives it a second comparative function: as one of the earliest-attested reflexes of the reconstructed [.smallcaps]#hero slay serpent# formula across the Indo-European daughter languages.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 46.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 46
The dossier thus stands at the intersection of two comparative axes — the Near Eastern cyclical-maintenance subtype and the Indo-European combat-formula tradition — both of which the Part II thematic chapters develop further.footnote:[Hoffner 1998, introduction to "Illuyanka"; Watkins 1995, ch. 46; Forsyth 1987, ch. 3.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=hoffner-1998 ; loc=Illuyanka introduction

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Egyptian (Ra-Apep) — the daily / nightly defeat of Apep in the Egyptian _Book of the Dead_ and the apotropaic Apep-spells is the other classic case of cyclical-maintenance chaoskampf; the Illuyanka / purulli annual structure and the Apep daily-cycle structure together define the cyclical-maintenance subtype developed in Part II C3.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Indo-European (Watkins formula) — Illuyanka is one of Watkins's four primary demonstration cases (with Vedic Vṛtra, Greek Python, and the Iranian Aži Dahāka materials) for the reconstructed HERO SLAY SERPENT formula; the lexical pairing _kuenta… illuyanka-_ is the Hittite reflex of _áhann áhim_; full development belongs to Part II C5.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Greek (Apollo's Septerion at Delphi) — the Septerion festival re-enacted the Python-slaying at Delphi on an eight-yearly cycle, providing a Greek cyclical-maintenance echo, though the Septerion is cult-foundation in flavour rather than sovereignty-renewal as at Nerik.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Norse (Thor / Jörmungandr at Ragnarök) — Version 2's loss-and-recovery pattern (heart and eyes taken and returned) has been compared to Thor's body-damage in the Ragnarök combat with Jörmungandr, though directly cognate only in very broad structural outlines; the comparison is best handled in the eschatological-displacement chapter (Part II C4) rather than as a tight Watkins-style reflex.
```

---

# Input file: chapters/18-thor-jormungandr.adoc

```
[[ch-18-thor-jormungandr]]
== Thor vs. Jörmungandr: Three Encounters and Ragnarök

Thor — the Norse storm-god — meets the world-encircling serpent Jörmungandr three times in the surviving record. He lifts the serpent disguised as a cat in the giant-king Útgarðaloki's hall. He hooks it on a baited line and is on the verge of striking the killing-blow when the giant Hymir cuts the line. He fights and kills it at Ragnarök, the doom of the gods, and dies of its venom in the same engagement. The three encounters are not three retellings of one combat but a layered record across three textual strata, three narrative-thematic registers, and a remarkable body of pre-Christian visual art.

=== Source overview

The chapter's two principal primary-source anchors are the *Poetic Edda* — a corpus of Old Norse mythological and heroic poems preserved chiefly in the late-thirteenth-century Icelandic *Codex Regius* (GKS 2365 4to) — and the *Prose Edda* (or *Snorra Edda*) of Snorri Sturluson, an Icelandic chieftain and historian who composed his prose handbook of mythology and skaldic poetics in the early thirteenth century, around 1220–1230 CE.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *The Poetic Edda*, revised Oxford World's Classics edition, introduction, on the Codex Regius and on the Eddic poetic corpus; Faulkes 1987, *Edda* by Snorri Sturluson, introduction, on Snorri's authorship and on the early-thirteenth-century date of the Prose Edda.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=introduction

The working English text for the *Poetic Edda* is Carolyne Larrington's revised translation, published as *The Poetic Edda* in the Oxford World's Classics series in 2014 (ISBN 9780199675340), which supplies the chapter's hymn-and-stanza-level English citations of the Eddic Thor-and-Jörmungandr material.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *The Poetic Edda*, revised edition, Oxford University Press, ISBN 9780199675340, used as the working English Eddic text; cited throughout at hymn-and-stanza level.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=publisher and edition

The working English text for the *Prose Edda* is Anthony Faulkes's translation, published as *Edda* (by Snorri Sturluson) in the Everyman's Library / Dent series in 1987 and reissued by the Viking Society for Northern Research in 2005 (ISBN 9780460876162), which supplies the chapter's chapter-level English citations of *Gylfaginning* and *Skáldskaparmál*.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Edda* by Snorri Sturluson, Everyman / Viking Society edition, ISBN 9780460876162, used as the working English Snorra Edda text; cited at chapter and page level.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=publisher and edition

Both editions are printed-only in the present project's working corpus, and the chapter cites them at hymn-and-stanza level (Larrington) and at chapter level (Faulkes) in line with the citation discipline applied throughout the Indo-European cluster of the present book.footnote:[Larrington 2014 and Faulkes 1987, both printed-only; cited at hymn-and-stanza and chapter level respectively per the established discipline of chapters 13, 14, and 15 of the present volume.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=printed-only edition

The chapter's Eddic primary text for Thor's fishing expedition is *Hymiskviða* ("The Lay of Hymir"), an Eddic poem of approximately forty stanzas in *fornyrðislag* metre that narrates Thor's journey to the giant Hymir's hall to fetch a brewing-cauldron and his rowing-out with Hymir to fish for the world-encircling serpent.footnote:[Larrington 2014, headnote and translation of *Hymiskviða*, on the poem's approximate length (around forty stanzas; the standard Neckel-Kuhn edition counts 38 complete stanzas plus one partial), its *fornyrðislag* metre, and its narrative arc from cauldron-quest to fishing expedition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Hymiskviða headnote

The chapter's Eddic primary text for the eschatological combat is *Völuspá* ("The Seeress's Prophecy"), the opening poem of the *Codex Regius* and the principal Eddic source for the Norse cosmogony and eschatology, in which the Thor-and-Jörmungandr Ragnarök combat is named and compressed in stanza 56 of the Larrington 2014 edition.footnote:[Larrington 2014, headnote and translation of *Völuspá* with attention to the Ragnarök sequence at stanzas 50–58, and specifically to stanza 56 on the Thor-and-Jörmungandr combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Völuspá headnote and st. 50–58

The chapter's Snorra Edda primary text is *Gylfaginning* ("The Tricking of Gylfi"), the second part of Snorri's handbook, which presents the Norse mythological corpus as a dialogue between the disguised gods Hárr, Jafnhárr, and Þriði and the visiting king Gylfi. Chapter 34 supplies the serpent's origin, chapters 46 to 48 supply the Útgarðaloki lifting-contest, and chapter 51 supplies the Ragnarök combat.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning*, on the dialogue-frame with Hárr / Jafnhárr / Þriði and Gylfi, and on the chapter-divisions used in the Faulkes edition; chapter 34 (the serpent's origin), chs. 46–48 (Útgarðaloki), ch. 51 (Ragnarök).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 34, 46–48, 51

The chapter's third primary-source stratum is the skaldic corpus — short occasional poems composed by named court-poets in the ninth, tenth, and eleventh centuries CE. The surviving skaldic verses on the fishing expedition include fragments by Bragi Boddason (early ninth century), Eysteinn Valdason (tenth century), and Úlfr Uggason (late tenth century, in his ekphrastic poem *Húsdrápa*), preserved chiefly through Snorri's quotations of them in *Skáldskaparmál*.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Skáldskaparmál*, on the skaldic verses on the Thor-and-Jörmungandr fishing expedition cited by Snorri, including fragments by Bragi Boddason, Eysteinn Valdason, and Úlfr Uggason; Faulkes 1987 introduction on the dating of the named skalds (Bragi 9th c.; Eysteinn and Úlfr 10th c.).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Skáldskaparmál (skaldic citations)

*Húsdrápa* is an ekphrastic skaldic poem composed by Úlfr Uggason in approximately 985 CE for the wedding-feast of Óláfr Höskuldsson at the hall of Hjarðarholt in western Iceland, in which the poet describes the carved or painted scenes on the panels of the hall — including a scene of Thor's fishing for Jörmungandr. The poem survives only as fragmentary quotations preserved in Snorri's *Skáldskaparmál*.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Skáldskaparmál*, headnote and citations for *Húsdrápa* by Úlfr Uggason, c. 985 CE, on the ekphrastic occasion (Hjarðarholt wedding-feast), on the surviving fragmentary quotations, and on the fishing scene depicted in the surviving stanzas.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Skáldskaparmál (Húsdrápa fragments)

The chapter's principal scholarly anchor for the Indo-European poetic dimension of the Thor-and-Jörmungandr combat is Calvert Watkins's *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics*, published by Oxford University Press in 1995, whose Part V treats the Old Norse skaldic and Eddic dragon-slaying diction as one of the principal Indo-European reflexes of the reconstructed HERO SLAY SERPENT poetic formula.footnote:[Watkins 1995, *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics*, Part V, on the Old Norse skaldic and Eddic dragon-slaying diction as a reflex of the reconstructed Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

Watkins 1995 is printed-only and is cited in the present chapter at section and chapter level (Part V), with the full philological argument on the skaldic and Eddic diction reserved for the comparative chapter on the Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (Part II C5 of the present book) and only summarised here.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V (printed only; cited at section and chapter level); per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the reservation of the full philological argument for Part II C5.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The chapter's principal scholarly anchor for the wider Indo-European storm-god / water-dragon complex is M. L. West's *Indo-European Poetry and Myth*, published by Oxford University Press in 2007, whose chapter 6 ("Storm and Stream") situates Thor as the Norse instance of the Indo-European storm-god type and treats the Thor-and-Jörmungandr combat alongside the Vedic Indra-Vṛtra, the Hittite Tarḫunna-Illuyanka, and the Avestan Thraētaona-Aži Dahāka comparanda.footnote:[West 2007, *Indo-European Poetry and Myth*, ch. 6 ("Storm and Stream"), on Thor as the Norse instance of the Indo-European storm-god / water-dragon complex and on the Thor-and-Jörmungandr combat in comparative Indo-European context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

West 2007 is also printed-only and is cited in the present chapter at chapter level (chapter 6, "Storm and Stream"), with the comparative arguments of chapter 6 supplying the wider Indo-European context for the Norse combat-narrative.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6 (printed only; cited at chapter level per the established discipline of chapters 13, 14, and 15 of the present volume).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The chapter's wider combat-myth comparative anchor is Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, published by Princeton University Press in 1987, which supplies a long-trajectory comparative survey of the combat-myth and includes treatment of the Norse Thor-and-Jörmungandr material in its chapter on the Indo-European combat-myth corpus.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, on the long-trajectory comparative survey including the Norse Thor-and-Jörmungandr material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=on Norse combat-myth

The four chief witnesses for the Thor-and-Jörmungandr corpus that the present chapter treats together are therefore the Eddic *Hymiskviða* (Larrington 2014), the Eddic *Völuspá* stanza 56 (Larrington 2014), the Snorra Edda *Gylfaginning* chapters 34, 46–48, and 51 (Faulkes 1987), and the skaldic *Húsdrápa* fragments preserved in *Skáldskaparmál* (Faulkes 1987), with Bragi Boddason and Eysteinn Valdason supplying further skaldic co-witnesses.footnote:[Larrington 2014 on *Hymiskviða* and *Völuspá*; Faulkes 1987 on *Gylfaginning* and on the *Skáldskaparmál* skaldic citations including *Húsdrápa*; per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the four-witness corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning and Skáldskaparmál

The Codex Regius is a late-thirteenth-century Icelandic manuscript (GKS 2365 4to) discovered in 1643 by Bishop Brynjólfur Sveinsson and now housed in the Árni Magnússon Institute for Icelandic Studies in Reykjavík. It is the principal manuscript witness to the Eddic poems including *Völuspá* (the opening poem) and *Hymiskviða* (the seventh poem of the mythological section).footnote:[Larrington 2014, introduction, on the Codex Regius (GKS 2365 4to), its discovery in 1643, its late-thirteenth-century date, and its position as the principal manuscript witness to the Eddic mythological corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=introduction (Codex Regius)

The Eddic poems preserved in the Codex Regius are widely held to be older in their compositional cores than the manuscript itself, with the principal mythological poems generally dated by their formulaic and lexical features to a Viking-Age compositional horizon between the ninth and the eleventh centuries CE — even where the precise dating of any individual poem remains contested.footnote:[Larrington 2014, introduction, on the dating of the Eddic mythological poems to a Viking-Age compositional horizon prior to the late-thirteenth-century manuscript and on the contested precision of poem-by-poem dating.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=introduction (Eddic dating)

The Snorra Edda is preserved in four principal medieval manuscripts — the Codex Upsaliensis (DG 11 4to, c. 1300), the Codex Regius of the Snorra Edda (GKS 2367 4to, c. 1325; not to be confused with the Eddic Codex Regius), the Codex Wormianus (AM 242 fol., c. 1340–1370), and the Codex Trajectinus (1593 paper transcript) — with Faulkes 1987 reading from the conflated text reconstructed across the four witnesses.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, introduction, on the four principal manuscripts of the Snorra Edda (Upsaliensis, Regius, Wormianus, Trajectinus) and on the conflated-text editorial procedure of the working English translation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=introduction (Snorra Edda manuscripts)

The chapter is organised around the brief's three-encounter structure: the Útgarðaloki lifting-contest of *Gylfaginning* 46–48, the Hymir fishing expedition (jointly attested by *Hymiskviða*, *Gylfaginning* 47–48 in narrative continuity with the lifting-contest, and the *Húsdrápa* fragments), and the Ragnarök combat of *Völuspá* 56 and *Gylfaginning* 51, with the three encounters treated as a layered record from different sources and registers rather than as alternative versions of a single underlying combat.footnote:[Per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the three-encounter structure as a layered record across the Eddic, skaldic, and Snorra Edda strata; per brief on the explicit "not alternative versions of one combat" framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 46–48, 51 (three-encounter structure)

The variant-handling classification for the present chapter is therefore *co-equal*: no single source has all three encounters fully narrated, and the chapter presents claims from each of the three textual strata in sequence with explicit source attribution rather than presenting one prevalent version with footnoted alternatives.footnote:[Per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml variants.classification: co-equal, with the three-encounter structure requiring Eddic, skaldic, and Snorra Edda witnesses together; Larrington 2014 and Faulkes 1987 on the three-encounter distribution across sources.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning (three-encounter co-equal classification)

The Thor-and-Jörmungandr corpus is unusually well-documented in pre-Christian Norse iconography. At least four surviving runestones or picture-stones depict the fishing expedition: the Altuna stone (Uppland, Sweden, c. 1050 CE), the Hørdum stone (Thy, Jutland, Denmark, c. 800 CE), the Gosforth fishing stone (Cumbria, England, c. 950 CE), and the Ardre VIII stone (Gotland, Sweden, c. 700–800 CE).footnote:[Per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml centrality_evidence on the four runestones and picture-stones depicting the fishing expedition; West 2007, ch. 6 ("Storm and Stream"), on the iconographic dossier; Faulkes 1987, introduction, on the iconographic supplement to the textual record.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (iconographic dossier)

The four-stone iconographic dossier is the most substantial surviving pre-Christian visual record of any single Norse mythological narrative, and it supplies an independent witness-stratum that pre-dates the Eddic and Snorra Edda manuscripts by between two and five centuries — with the earliest stone (Ardre VIII) dating to roughly the eighth century CE and the latest (Altuna) to the eleventh century CE.footnote:[Per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml iconography centrality on the pre-Christian iconographic dossier as an independent witness-stratum predating the manuscript record; West 2007, ch. 6, on the dating range and significance of the four-stone series.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6 (iconographic chronology)

The chapter's principal *lacunae* are two. First, *Völuspá* 56 compresses the Ragnarök combat into a few lines of mythic-cosmic verse, leaving the bulk of narrative detail to be supplied by Snorri's *Gylfaginning* 51. Second, *Húsdrápa* survives only as fragmentary skaldic quotations preserved in Snorri's *Skáldskaparmál*, with the original ekphrastic poem otherwise lost.footnote:[Per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml lacunae section on the Völuspá 56 compression and the *Húsdrápa* fragmentary preservation; Larrington 2014 on the Völuspá 56 compression; Faulkes 1987 on the *Húsdrápa* fragmentary preservation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Völuspá 56; Faulkes 1987 Skáldskaparmál

Both lacunae are *in-tradition fillable* under the chapter's reconstruction policy: the *Völuspá* 56 compression is filled from *Gylfaginning* 51 (a Norse in-tradition source), and the *Húsdrápa* fragmentary preservation is filled from Snorri's own preservation of the surviving stanzas in *Skáldskaparmál* (also a Norse in-tradition source), with no cross-tradition reconstruction permitted under the present book's policy.footnote:[Per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml lacunae in-tradition-fills-available; per chaoskampf scope.md §"Reconstruction policy" on the no-cross-tradition reconstruction rule and on the donor-source naming requirement; Faulkes 1987 *Gylfaginning* 51 (Ragnarök expansion) and *Skáldskaparmál* (Húsdrápa preservation).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning 51 and Skáldskaparmál (in-tradition fills)

The Snorri-as-witness problem is itself a recurring methodological theme in modern Norse scholarship. Snorri composed the Snorra Edda in the early thirteenth century, more than two hundred years after the official conversion of Iceland to Christianity in 999/1000 CE, and his handbook is at once the fullest synthesis of Norse mythology and a Christian-period work whose framing-narrative — Gylfi's interrogation of the disguised "Æsir" — presents the Norse gods as deceivers and his treatment as antiquarian rather than confessional.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, introduction, on the early-thirteenth-century date of the Snorra Edda, on the conversion of Iceland to Christianity in 999/1000 CE, and on Snorri's antiquarian-rather-than-confessional framing of the Norse mythological material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=introduction (Snorri-as-witness)

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reliance on Snorri as the principal narrative-continuous source, while supplementing him with the older Eddic poems and the still-older skaldic fragments, is itself a methodological commitment about how to read the Norse mythological corpus, distinct from any specific philological or comparative claim within the corpus; the method is well-supported by Faulkes 1987's editorial practice and by the brief's three-encounter framing, but the chapter flags it explicitly because the resulting "Thor-and-Jörmungandr corpus" is a synthesis of textual strata of different ages and registers, with Snorri's thirteenth-century Christian-period prose reading the older Viking-Age and pagan-period verse. | basis: Faulkes 1987 editorial practice; brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml three-encounter framing; Larrington 2014 on the Eddic-poem dating; Watkins 1995 on the skaldic dating | risk: Snorri is read as a witness to older traditions whose reception he himself shaped, and the chapter's three-encounter synthesis is therefore an editorial reading of layered evidence rather than a straightforward report of any single source.]

The chapter does not attempt to harmonise the three encounters into a single coherent biographical narrative for either Thor or Jörmungandr. Each encounter is treated in its own narrative-and-source context, and apparent discontinuities — the cat-disguise of *Gylfaginning* 47, the line-cut of *Hymiskviða* 23–24, the apparent beheading of the *Húsdrápa* fragments, the mutual killing of *Völuspá* 56 and *Gylfaginning* 51 — are presented as features of the layered record rather than as contradictions to be resolved.footnote:[Per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml special_instructions on the layered-record framing and on the explicit non-harmonisation; Faulkes 1987 *Gylfaginning* chs. 46–48 and 51; Larrington 2014 *Hymiskviða* and *Völuspá*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning (non-harmonisation framing)

=== Setting and Norse mythological context

Thor (Old Norse Þórr) is the storm-god of the Norse pantheon, son of Óðinn (the chief of the Æsir) and of the giantess Jörð ("Earth"), and the principal warrior-figure of the Æsir. His function in the Norse mythological corpus is the defence of Midgard (Old Norse Miðgarðr) — the world of humans — against the giants and against the great adversaries of the cosmos.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* chs. 9 and 21, on Thor (Þórr) as son of Óðinn and Jörð, on his position as principal warrior of the Æsir, and on his role as defender of Miðgarðr against the giants and against the great adversaries.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 9 and 21

Thor is one of the most frequently named gods in the surviving Norse mythological corpus and the recipient of the largest body of skaldic praise-poetry on combat themes. Named Viking-Age skalds — Bragi Boddason in the early ninth century, Eysteinn Valdason in the tenth century, Úlfr Uggason in the late tenth century, and others — composed verse on Thor's combats with the giants and with Jörmungandr.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Skáldskaparmál*, on the named Viking-Age skalds composing on Thor's combats and on the volume of skaldic Thor-praise relative to other gods; Watkins 1995 Part V on the skaldic Thor-corpus as a body of named-poet attestations.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Skáldskaparmál (skaldic Thor-corpus)

Thor's combat-weapon is Mjöllnir (normalised Old Norse Mjǫllnir), a hammer forged by the dwarves Brokkr and Eitri (or Sindri) in the contest-and-bargain narrative of *Skáldskaparmál* 35 — the "smiths' contest" episode — with the hammer described as so potent that it never fails of its target and returns to the wielder's hand after striking.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Skáldskaparmál* ch. 35 (the smiths' contest), on the forging of Mjöllnir by the dwarves Brokkr and Eitri/Sindri and on the hammer's properties of unfailing strike and return-to-hand.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Skáldskaparmál ch. 35

Mjöllnir is the cosmic combat-weapon of the Norse storm-god in the same structural position as Indra's *vajra* in the Vedic combat-corpus and Baal's maces Ayyamur and Yagrush in the Ugaritic Baal Cycle: a divinely forged implement whose use by the storm-god is the materialised image of the lightning-strike and the principal mythological signifier of the storm-god's combat-function.footnote:[West 2007, ch. 6 ("Storm and Stream"), on Mjöllnir's structural position alongside the Vedic *vajra* and the Ugaritic Ayyamur/Yagrush as the materialised combat-implement of the Indo-European storm-god; Faulkes 1987 *Gylfaginning* 21 on Mjöllnir's combat-function.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

Thor wears two further combat-implements alongside Mjöllnir: a belt of strength (Old Norse *Megingjǫrð*) that doubles his already great power when girded, and a pair of iron gauntlets without which he cannot grip the hammer's short haft. The three implements together constitute the Norse storm-god's combat-equipment set.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 21, on Thor's combat-equipment set (Mjöllnir, *Megingjǫrð*, iron gauntlets) and on the doubling of his strength when girded.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 21

Thor's chariot is drawn by two goats — Tanngnjóstr ("Tooth-gnasher") and Tanngrisnir ("Tooth-grinder") — whose hooves sound the thunder, in a chariot-and-thunder image that aligns Thor with the chariot-borne storm-gods of the wider Indo-European tradition.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 21, on Thor's chariot, the two named goats, and the thunder-image; West 2007, ch. 6, on the chariot-and-thunder image as a wider Indo-European storm-god feature.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 21

Óðinn (Old Norse Óðinn, sometimes anglicised "Odin" but kept here in the Old Norse spelling per the chapter's glossary discipline) is the chief of the Æsir, the father of Thor, the ruler of Valhǫll, and the patron-god of poetry and war. His role in the Thor-and-Jörmungandr corpus is largely structural — as the cosmic-rulership figure under whom Thor's defensive function is exercised — rather than directly narrative.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* chs. 9, 20, on Óðinn as chief of the Æsir, father of Thor, and ruler of Valhǫll; per brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml glossary discipline on the Óðinn (locked spelling) form.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 9 and 20

Loki is the Norse trickster-god, sometimes counted among the Æsir and sometimes treated as a giant ("of the giant kindred"), and his role in the Thor-and-Jörmungandr corpus is generative: Jörmungandr is Loki's son by the giantess Angrboða, born alongside the wolf Fenrir and the goddess Hel, in the lineage-narrative recorded in *Gylfaginning* 34.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 34, on Loki as the trickster-god, on Loki's union with Angrboða, and on Jörmungandr as Loki's son alongside Fenrir and Hel.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 34

Jörmungandr (Old Norse Jǫrmungandr, also called Miðgarðsormr, "the Midgard Serpent") is the world-encircling serpent of Norse cosmology, lying in the ocean that surrounds Midgard with its tail in its mouth, and is the principal serpentine adversary of Thor in the Norse combat-corpus.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 34, on Jörmungandr (Miðgarðsormr) as the world-encircling serpent lying in the surrounding ocean with its tail in its mouth; Larrington 2014 on the Miðgarðsormr designation in the Eddic corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 34

The Miðgarðsormr designation ("Midgard-serpent") encodes the serpent's cosmic-spatial function directly in its name. Miðgarðr is the "middle enclosure" — the world of humans — and the serpent's role is the encirclement of this world, with its body forming the outer boundary of the human cosmos and the inner edge of the surrounding ocean.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* chs. 8 and 34, on the cosmographic structure of Miðgarðr as the "middle enclosure" surrounded by the ocean and on the Miðgarðsormr as the serpent encircling that ocean.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 8 and 34

The Norse cosmography is structured by a tripartite vertical-and-horizontal scheme: the world-tree Yggdrasil at the centre, with Ásgarðr (the realm of the gods) above, Midgard (the world of humans) at the middle level surrounded by the ocean and the encircling serpent, and Útgarðr (the "outer enclosure", realm of the giants) and Niflheimr / Hel beyond.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* chs. 8, 14, 34, on the tripartite Norse cosmography (Ásgarðr, Miðgarðr, Útgarðr / Niflheimr) organised around Yggdrasil and on the encircling-serpent's position at the boundary between Miðgarðr and the outer realms.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 8, 14, 34

The encircling serpent's cosmic-boundary position has the consequence that any combat between Thor and Jörmungandr is structurally a combat at and for the boundary of the human world. The serpent's defeat unmakes the boundary, and the boundary's unmaking is the structural feature that makes the Ragnarök combat the eschatological close of the Norse cosmic age.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* chs. 34 and 51, on the cosmic-boundary position of Jörmungandr and on the eschatological consequence of his defeat at Ragnarök; West 2007 ch. 6 on the cosmic-boundary structure as a feature of the Norse instance of the storm-god / water-dragon complex.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 34 and 51

The lineage-and-binding narrative of *Gylfaginning* 34 also records that Óðinn, learning of the threat that Loki's three monstrous offspring posed to the gods, dispatched them to three different cosmic stations: Hel was sent to rule the realm of the dead (Hel / Niflheimr), Fenrir was bound by an unbreakable chain (Gleipnir) on a remote island, and Jörmungandr was thrown into the ocean encircling Midgard, where the serpent grew so large that it could encircle the world and bite its own tail.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 34, on Óðinn's dispatch of Loki's three offspring to their three cosmic stations and on Jörmungandr's growth in the ocean to encircle the world and bite its own tail.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 34

The dispatching-and-encircling narrative of *Gylfaginning* 34 is also the explicit aetiology of the serpent's *position*: Jörmungandr is in the ocean because Óðinn put it there, and the encircling-of-the-world is the consequence of the serpent's growth from the giant-progeny that Óðinn could not destroy outright.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 34, on the aetiology of Jörmungandr's encircling position as consequent on Óðinn's dispatching of the giant-progeny he could not destroy.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 34

That dispatching-and-encircling narrative also places Thor's eventual mortal combat with Jörmungandr at Ragnarök inside a longer-term structural logic. The gods could not destroy the serpent at its origin, so the serpent's destruction is deferred to the end of the cosmic age, when the deferred combat releases the serpent's accumulated threat and ends the Æsir's regime.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* chs. 34 and 51, on the structural-logic relation between the original dispatching-and-encircling and the eventual mutual killing at Ragnarök; West 2007 ch. 6 on the deferred-combat structural feature.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 34 and 51

The Æsir / Vanir / giant tripartite division of Norse mythological agency places Thor's combats firmly in the Æsir-versus-giants axis: Thor is the Æsir's principal combatant against the giants, and the Jötnar (giants) — including Hymir, Útgarðaloki, and Loki's giant-side kin — are the chronic adversaries against whom Thor's hammer is repeatedly raised.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* chs. 1, 9, 21, on the tripartite Æsir / Vanir / giant division and on Thor's principal combat-axis as Æsir-versus-giants.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 1, 9, 21

Hymir is a giant of the Norse mythological corpus whose principal narrative role is as the host (or unwilling host) of Thor in *Hymiskviða* and as Thor's companion (or rival) in the fishing expedition for Jörmungandr. In *Gylfaginning* Hymir is named as the boat-owner and rower; in *Hymiskviða* he appears as the resident giant whose cauldron the Æsir need for their feast-brewing.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Hymiskviða* headnote and translation, on Hymir as the resident giant and host-rival of the cauldron-quest; Faulkes 1987 *Gylfaginning* ch. 47, on Hymir as the boat-owner and rower in the fishing expedition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Hymiskviða headnote

Útgarðaloki is the giant-king of Útgarðr ("the outer enclosure", the realm of the giants), and his principal narrative role in the Norse corpus is as the trickster-host of Thor and his companions in the elaborate visit-to-Útgarðr episode of *Gylfaginning* 45 to 47, in which the giant-king's illusions are progressively unmasked across a sequence of contests.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* chs. 45–47, on Útgarðaloki as the giant-king of Útgarðr, on his trickster-host function, and on the visit-to-Útgarðr episode's sequence of contests.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 45–47

Ragnarök (Old Norse Ragnarǫk) is the Norse eschatological end, the "doom of the gods", at which the present cosmic age closes in a sequence of cosmic disasters and final combats: Surtr the fire-giant burns the world, the wolf Fenrir slays Óðinn, the wolf Skǫll (or Hati) swallows the sun, and Thor and Jörmungandr meet for the last and mutual combat that the present chapter's third encounter narrates.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Völuspá* sts. 50–58, on the Ragnarök sequence; Faulkes 1987 *Gylfaginning* ch. 51 on the Ragnarök narrative including the Thor-and-Jörmungandr mutual combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Völuspá sts. 50–58

The Ragnarök eschatological structure is followed in the Eddic and Snorra Edda corpus by a renewal-narrative — a "new earth" rising green from the sea, a small surviving group of gods (including Thor's sons Móði and Magni, who inherit Mjöllnir), and the return of certain figures — but the Thor-and-Jörmungandr mutual killing is itself the close of the present cosmic age and not a feature of the renewal.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Völuspá* sts. 59–66, on the post-Ragnarök renewal; Faulkes 1987 *Gylfaginning* chs. 52–53 on the renewal-narrative including the surviving gods and the inheritance of Mjöllnir; Faulkes 1987 ch. 51 on the mutual killing as close-of-age.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Völuspá sts. 59–66

The Norse combat-myth is therefore distinct in its eschatological structure from the cyclical-maintenance combat of the Egyptian Apep tradition (chapter 16 of the present volume) and from the once-only cosmogonic combat of the Mesopotamian Marduk-Tiamat tradition (chapter 3 of the present volume). The Thor-and-Jörmungandr combat is once-only and end-of-age rather than recurrent or origin-of-age, and the Norse eschatological position is the principal subtype-feature of the Thor-and-Jörmungandr corpus.footnote:[Per chaoskampf scope.md §"Subject definition" on the four combat-myth subtypes (cosmogonic / sovereignty / cyclical-maintenance / eschatological) and on the Thor-and-Jörmungandr position in the eschatological category; Faulkes 1987 *Gylfaginning* ch. 51 and Larrington 2014 *Völuspá* st. 56 on the once-only end-of-age structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 51

=== Encounter 1 — Útgarðaloki and the lifting of the cat (Gylfaginning 46–48)

The first of the three encounters is the lifting-of-the-cat trickery in the visit-to-Útgarðr narrative of *Gylfaginning* 45 to 47 (with consequence in chapter 48), in which Thor — accompanied by Loki and the human servants Þjálfi and Rǫskva — visits the giant-king Útgarðaloki's hall and is set a series of contests of strength, agility, and consumption.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* chs. 45–47, on the visit-to-Útgarðr narrative, on Thor's companions (Loki, Þjálfi, Rǫskva), on the giant-king Útgarðaloki, and on the contest-sequence in his hall.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning chs. 45–47

The visit-to-Útgarðr is preceded in *Gylfaginning* 45 by the night-in-the-glove episode, in which Thor and his companions sleep inside what they take to be a great hall and discover at dawn that they have spent the night inside the discarded glove of the giant Skrýmir, whose snoring Thor had attempted (and failed) to silence with three hammer-blows that left only small dents in the giant's brow.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 45, on the night-in-the-glove episode, on Skrýmir as the giant whose glove they sleep in, and on Thor's three hammer-blows that fail to wake or kill the giant.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 45

Skrýmir is later revealed (in the post-departure exposition of *Gylfaginning* 47) to have been Útgarðaloki himself in disguise, and the giant-king's confession that he had interposed a mountain between Thor's hammer and his own brow — leaving only the dented mountain as evidence — establishes the trickster-illusion register of the entire visit-to-Útgarðr sequence.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 47, on the post-departure revelation that Skrýmir was Útgarðaloki in disguise and on the mountain-interposition trick that produced the dented mountain in place of the wounded giant.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 47

The Útgarðr contests proper occupy *Gylfaginning* 46, with each member of Thor's party set a contest against an apparent opponent: Loki competes in eating against Logi ("Wildfire"), Þjálfi races against Hugi ("Thought"), Thor attempts to drain a horn of mead, Thor attempts to lift Útgarðaloki's grey cat, and Thor wrestles with the old woman Elli ("Old Age").footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 46, on the contest-sequence in Útgarðaloki's hall: Loki vs. Logi (eating); Þjálfi vs. Hugi (running); Thor and the horn (drinking); Thor and the cat (lifting); Thor and Elli (wrestling).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 46

The horn-drinking contest is presented as a test of capacity. Útgarðaloki sets Thor a drinking-horn that he claims his retainers can empty in one or at most three draughts, and Thor — in three great pulls — succeeds only in lowering the level of the horn by what appears to the eye to be a small amount, despite his efforts.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 46, on the horn-drinking contest, on Útgarðaloki's claim about retainer-capacity, and on Thor's three pulls that lower the level only slightly.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 46

The lifting-of-the-cat contest immediately follows the horn-drinking and is presented as a test of strength. Útgarðaloki produces a large grey cat and challenges Thor to lift it from the hall floor, and Thor — putting his arms under the cat's belly and heaving with all his strength — succeeds only in raising the cat so far that one of its paws leaves the ground.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 46, on the lifting-of-the-cat contest, on Útgarðaloki's grey cat as the object to be lifted, and on Thor's heave that raises only one of the cat's paws from the floor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 46

Thor's apparent failure at the cat-lift is recorded by *Gylfaginning* 46 as causing visible mockery from the giant-king's retainers. The failure is the second of three apparent humiliations (after the horn) that Thor undergoes within the giant-king's hall: the wrestling-match with Elli completes the trio.footnote:[Faulkes 1987, *Gylfaginning* ch. 46, on the mockery of Thor's failure at the cat-lift and on the three-humiliations sequence (horn, cat, Elli) within the giant-king's hall.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=faulkes-snorri ; loc=Gylfaginning ch. 46
