// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The principal primary texts for the Welsh Red-and-White Dragon sub-case are two: the Latin *Historia Brittonum* (the so-called Nennius-recension, conventionally dated c. 828 CE) and the Middle Welsh prose tale *Lludd and Llefelys*, transmitted in the *Mabinogion* manuscripts (the White Book of Rhydderch c. 1350 CE and the Red Book of Hergest c. 1382–1410 CE), with both texts cited at chapter / episode level in the present chapter and the principal scholarly anchor Daniel Ogden's *Drakōn* chapter on Celtic material.footnote:[*Historia Brittonum* c. 828 CE (Nennius recension); *Lludd and Llefelys* in the *Mabinogion*, principal manuscript witnesses White Book of Rhydderch c. 1350 CE and Red Book of Hergest c. 1382–1410 CE; Ogden 2013, *Drakōn: Dragon Myth and Serpent Cult in the Greek and Roman Worlds*, ch. on Celtic material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

Ogden 2013 is printed-only and is cited at chapter level in the present chapter, with the specific chapter-locus the volume's Celtic-material chapter; this is in line with the established citation discipline for Ogden 2013 across chapter 12 of the present book.footnote:[Ogden 2013, ch. on Celtic material (printed only; cited at chapter level per the established discipline).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

The principal primary text for the Batraz sub-case is the Ossetic Nart saga corpus, transmitted as oral epic recorded in the 19th and 20th centuries from Ossetian, Circassian, and Chechen narrators, with the principal scholarly anchor Georges Dumézil's twentieth-century reconstructions reading Batraz as the Scytho-Alanic Indo-European warrior / dragon-slayer reflex; the chapter cites the Nart material at corpus level rather than at specific-edition level per the brief's identifier_missing flag for the Batraz material.footnote:[Ossetic Nart saga corpus, 19th- and 20th-century recordings from Ossetian, Circassian, and Chechen narrators; West 2007, ch. 6, on Batraz as Scytho-Alanic IE warrior / dragon-slayer reflex following Dumézil's twentieth-century reading; cited at corpus level per inventory.approved.yaml identifier_missing flag for the batraz-nart entry.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The principal primary text for the Vahagn sub-case is Movses Khorenatsi's *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* ("History of the Armenians"), conventionally dated to the 5th to 9th century CE in successive scholarly assessments, whose book I chapter 31 supplies the standard locus for Khorenatsi's late paraphrase of an earlier Armenian hymn to Vahagn — a hymn whose text itself is lost — with the principal scholarly anchor James R. Russell's *Zoroastrianism in Armenia* (Harvard Iranian Series 5, 1987).footnote:[Movses Khorenatsi, *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* book I (with the Vahagn paraphrase at I.31); composition conventionally dated 5th–9th c. CE in successive scholarly assessments; Russell 1987, *Zoroastrianism in Armenia* (Harvard Iranian Series 5; ISBN 9780674968509), Vahagn chapter; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the fragmentary status of the Vahagn corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

Russell 1987 is printed-only and is cited at chapter level in the present chapter, with the specific chapter-locus the volume's Vahagn chapter; the chapter's Vahagn discussion is presented with explicit fragmentary framing throughout, since the hymn-text itself is lost and what survives is only Khorenatsi's late paraphrase.footnote:[Russell 1987, Vahagn chapter (printed only; cited at chapter level per the established discipline); per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the explicit fragmentary framing required for the Vahagn material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

The chapter's secondary survey-anchor is Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, published by Princeton University Press in 1987, whose treatment of the Indo-European combat-myth supplies a wider scholarly anchor for the long-arc framing of the chapter's six sub-cases as reflexes of a single inherited combat-pattern.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, treatment of Indo-European combat-myth as background to the six sub-cases of the present chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=on IE combat-myth

The chapter is a family-chapter (related-tier) treatment, organising six Indo-European dragon-combat sub-cases by their position on a philological-evidence spectrum — from the strongest philological evidence (the Old Norse Eddic Sigurd-Fáfnir corpus where Watkins reads the diction as a direct IE-formula reflex) to the most fragmentary (the Armenian Vahagn material attested only through Khorenatsi's late paraphrase of a lost hymn) — and the six sub-cases are treated in their own clearly-bounded sub-sections that are not merged at the level of either narrative content or comparative framing.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the family-chapter framing with six sub-cases organised on a philological-evidence spectrum; Watkins 1995 ch. 42 (Sigurd as strongest philological evidence); Russell 1987 (Vahagn as most fragmentary).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

The chapter's overall argument is that these six narratives — Sigurd's killing of Fáfnir, Beowulf's killings of Grendel and the hoard-dragon, Lugh's killing of Balor at the Second Battle of Mag Tuired, the Welsh Red-and-White Dragon combat, Batraz's combats in the Nart saga, and Vahagn's killing of the *vishaps* — are the Indo-European reflexes that the Watkins HERO SLAY SERPENT formula covers, with the chapter feeding directly into Part II C5 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the IE formula) as its main feeder.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the chapter as the main feeder to Part II C5; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the consolidated IE-reflex argument; West 2007, ch. 6, on the wider IE storm-god / water-dragon complex.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

=== Sigurd vs. Fáfnir — strongest philological evidence

The Sigurd-Fáfnir narrative is preserved principally in the Eddic poem *Fáfnismál* in the Codex Regius (GKS 2365 4to, c. 1270 CE), one of three closely-grouped poems of the Sigurd-cycle in the Codex (with *Reginsmál* preceding and *Sigrdrífumál* following in the manuscript order), and is also attested in the prose *Völsunga saga* (c. 1270 CE) and in the Middle High German *Nibelungenlied* (c. 1200 CE) as the related Germanic witness with Sigurd in the German form Siegfried.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* and the surrounding Codex Regius poems (*Reginsmál* preceding, *Sigrdrífumál* following) on Sigurd; *Völsunga saga* c. 1270 CE; *Nibelungenlied* c. 1200 CE as the related Middle High German Siegfried witness; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the related-Germanic-witness status of the Nibelungenlied.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál

Sigurd is the son of Sigmund of the Völsung lineage, raised in the household of the smith Regin after his father's death, and the *Fáfnismál* presents him as a young hero on the threshold of his first major heroic deed, the killing of the dragon Fáfnir on the heath of Gnitaheiðr.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* and the surrounding Codex Regius poems (with *Reginsmál* on Sigurd's upbringing and Regin's role); on the Völsung lineage and Gnitaheiðr setting.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Reginsmál; Fáfnismál

Fáfnir is a dwarf transformed into a dragon by his guardianship of the cursed gold of Andvari, gold that Loki had earlier extorted from the dwarf Andvari to pay a wergild to Hreidmar (Fáfnir's and Regin's father) for the killing of Otr, and the curse on the gold is the load-bearing motif that drives the Sigurd-cycle from the killing of the dragon to Sigurd's own death.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Reginsmál* and *Fáfnismál*, on the cursed gold of Andvari, Loki's extortion of the hoard, the wergild for Otr, Fáfnir's transformation into a dragon, and the curse-driven trajectory of the cycle; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the cursed-gold and Sigurd-death motifs.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Reginsmál; Fáfnismál

Regin, Fáfnir's brother and the smith who fosters Sigurd, forges the sword Gram from the fragments of Sigmund's broken sword and incites Sigurd to ambush Fáfnir on the heath, and the *Fáfnismál* opens with Sigurd at the moment of the killing-stroke, having dug a pit on the dragon's path to the water and lying in wait below.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Reginsmál* on the forging of Gram from Sigmund's sword-fragments and Regin's incitement; *Fáfnismál* opening on the pit-and-ambush mechanism.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál opening

The killing-stroke itself is delivered with the sword Gram from the pit: Sigurd thrusts upward into Fáfnir's underbelly as the dragon passes over the pit on his way to the water, and the *Fáfnismál* presents the killing-stroke as the central act around which the poem's dialogue is organised rather than as an extended combat-narrative.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* on Sigurd's upward thrust with Gram from the pit and on the structure of the poem as dialogue around the central killing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál

The post-killing dialogue between Sigurd and the dying Fáfnir occupies the bulk of the *Fáfnismál*: Fáfnir, mortally wounded, asks Sigurd's name, warns him of the curse on the gold, and supplies cosmological-and-genealogical lore in the wisdom-dialogue register that the *Vafþrúðnismál* and *Grímnismál* establish elsewhere in the Eddic corpus.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál*, on the post-killing wisdom-dialogue between Sigurd and Fáfnir, including the curse-warning and the cosmological-and-genealogical exchange; on the Eddic wisdom-dialogue register paralleled in *Vafþrúðnismál* and *Grímnismál*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál stanzas (post-killing dialogue)

After Fáfnir's death Regin requests that Sigurd roast the dragon's heart, and when Sigurd touches the cooking heart with his finger and licks the burned blood from his finger he gains understanding of the speech of birds, and the nuthatches in the surrounding trees warn him that Regin plans to kill him for the treasure.footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* on the heart-roasting, the blood-tasting, the finger-burn, the bird-speech understanding, and the nuthatches' warning; on the resulting threat from Regin.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál (heart-roasting and bird-speech)

Sigurd, warned by the birds, kills Regin in turn — beheading him with Gram — and then takes Fáfnir's hoard onto his horse Grani, with the *Fáfnismál* ending on Sigurd in possession of the cursed gold and on the way to the next stage of the cycle (his encounter with the valkyrie Sigrdrífa / Brynhild in the *Sigrdrífumál*).footnote:[Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* concluding stanzas and *Sigrdrífumál* opening, on Sigurd's killing of Regin, the loading of the hoard onto Grani, and the bridge to the Sigrdrífa / Brynhild encounter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál concluding stanzas; Sigrdrífumál opening

The Sigurd-cycle's outcome is ambiguous in a way that distinguishes it from the cosmogonic and sovereignty-establishment subtypes of the combat myth: Sigurd kills the dragon and gains the hoard, but the curse on the gold drives the long-arc tragedy of the cycle (Brynhild's death, the Burgundian disasters, Sigurd's own death at the hands of Gunnar's brother Gutthorm) and the killing therefore destroys rather than secures the hero's prosperity.footnote:[Larrington 2014, on the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle from *Fáfnismál* through *Sigrdrífumál* and the Burgundian poems to Sigurd's death; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the ambiguous-outcome reading and on the forward-link to Part II C7 (inversion problem).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd-cycle long arc

[INFERENCE: the reading of the Sigurd-cycle's outcome as ambiguous — the killing brings the hero the treasure but also the curse that will destroy him — is the chapter's editorial reading of the long-arc tragedy of the cycle, drawn from Larrington 2014 on the Codex Regius poems and from Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on the diction of the killing, and is presented in the chapter as the principal forward-link to Part II C7 of the present book (the inversion problem); the *Fáfnismál* itself does not in so many words frame the killing as destroying-rather-than-securing prosperity, but the curse-warning of Fáfnir to Sigurd and the long-arc trajectory of the surrounding Eddic poems supply the basis for the inference. | basis: Larrington 2014 on the curse-driven trajectory of the Sigurd-cycle including Fáfnir's curse-warning at *Fáfnismál*; Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on the philological diction of the killing; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the C7-inversion forward-link | risk: the framing as "ambiguous outcome" is a comparative-typological reading rather than a direct attestation, and the *Fáfnismál* presents the killing as a heroic deed in its own right within the poem's own register.]

The Sigurd-cycle is also attested in the prose *Völsunga saga* (c. 1270 CE), which gives a continuous-narrative version of the cycle including the Fáfnir-killing episode, and the prose witness is treated by Old Norse scholarship as preserving older oral-tradition material alongside the Eddic poetic witnesses; the present chapter cites the *Völsunga saga* as a parallel witness rather than as the primary text for the *Fáfnismál* episode itself.footnote:[*Völsunga saga* c. 1270 CE as continuous-narrative parallel witness to the Sigurd-cycle including the Fáfnir-killing episode; Larrington 2014 on the Eddic-and-prose witness relationship.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd-cycle parallel witnesses

The Middle High German *Nibelungenlied* (c. 1200 CE) is the related Germanic witness to the Sigurd-cycle, with Sigurd in the German form Siegfried and the dragon-killing transposed into a brief retrospective narrative episode rather than treated as a focal scene of the poem; the *Nibelungenlied* is cited in the present chapter at corpus level as the related Germanic witness rather than as a primary text for the Fáfnir-episode.footnote:[*Nibelungenlied* c. 1200 CE on Siegfried's dragon-killing in retrospective narrative; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the related-Germanic-witness status of the Nibelungenlied as parallel to the Old Norse Sigurd material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd / Siegfried Germanic parallel

In Watkins's reading in *How to Kill a Dragon* chapter 42, the Eddic *Fáfnismál* diction of Sigurd's killing is a direct reflex of the reconstructed Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula: the verb of killing (Old Norse *vega*, "to kill in combat"), its subject (the named hero), and its object (the named dragon) instantiate in Old Norse the same syntactic-and-semantic structure that Vedic *áhann áhim* ("he slew the serpent") instantiates in Vedic Sanskrit and that Hittite *kuenta . . . illuyanka* ("he killed the serpent") instantiates in Hittite.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the *Fáfnismál* diction of Sigurd's killing as a direct reflex of the reconstructed IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula; Vedic *áhann áhim* (RV 1.32) and Hittite *kuenta . . . illuyanka* (CTH 321) as the comparative philological exhibits of the same formula.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

Watkins's argument in chapter 42 is that the *Fáfnismál* preserves the IE poetic formula at the level of the verbal diction of the killing-stroke itself, not merely at the level of the structural-typological pattern, and that the Norse sub-case is therefore among the strongest philological evidence in the post-Iranian, post-Vedic, post-Anatolian corpus for the reconstructed formula's status as Indo-European poetic inheritance rather than as accidental cross-tradition convergence.footnote:[Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the philological strength of the Norse evidence as inheritance rather than convergence; on the *Fáfnismál* diction of the killing-stroke as the load-bearing locus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

The Sigurd-Fáfnir episode is also one of the iconographically densest in the post-Vedic IE corpus, with several extant Scandinavian rune-stones and rock carvings from the 11th and 12th centuries CE depicting Sigurd's killing of Fáfnir (the Ramsund carving in Södermanland c. 1030 CE is the principal-and-best-preserved witness), and the iconographic dossier supplements the Eddic and prose textual witnesses as a third register of attestation alongside the verbal-narrative and the philological-formulaic.footnote:[Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on the Sigurd-Fáfnir iconographic tradition as a register of attestation alongside the Eddic and prose witnesses; Ramsund carving (Södermanland c. 1030 CE) as principal Scandinavian witness; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the iconographic dossier as a third register of attestation; specific stone-count to be pinned at glossary-lock against a rune-stone-specialist source.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42 (Sigurd-Fáfnir iconographic dossier)

[INFERENCE: the identification of the Sigurd-Fáfnir Eddic-iconographic-philological triple-witness pattern as parallel to the Thor-Jörmungandr triple-witness pattern of chapter 18 of the present volume is a comparative reading drawn from the wider Norse evidence, presented as a parallel between two Old Norse cases rather than as a feature of either case in isolation; the parallel is presented in the chapter as a comparative observation rather than as a direct claim of the *Fáfnismál* itself. | basis: Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on the *Fáfnismál*-diction strength; Larrington 2014 on the Sigurd-iconographic dossier; brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the Thor triple-witness pattern; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the back-link to Ch. 18 | risk: the parallel is typological-comparative rather than evidence of direct connection between the two cycles.]

=== Beowulf vs. Grendel and the dragon

The Old English poem *Beowulf* survives in a single manuscript witness — London, British Library, Cotton Vitellius A.xv (the Nowell Codex), c. 1000 CE — and is conventionally assigned to a composition-period bracketed somewhere in the 7th to 10th centuries CE, with the dragon-episode forming the third and final movement of the poem after the Grendel-episode and the Grendel's-mother-episode that dominate the poem's first two movements.footnote:[*Beowulf*, single-manuscript witness London BL Cotton Vitellius A.xv (Nowell Codex), c. 1000 CE; composition 7th–10th c. CE in standard scholarly bracketing; three-movement structure (Grendel / Grendel's mother / dragon) with the dragon-episode as third and final movement; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the manuscript and composition-dating conventions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

Beowulf is a Geatish hero — a member of the Gēatas, a southern Scandinavian people — who in the poem's first movement travels to the Danish court of king Hrothgar to defend the hall Heorot from the man-eating monster Grendel, the descendant of Cain who has been killing Hrothgar's warriors at Heorot for twelve years.footnote:[*Beowulf*, the Geatish-arrival passage (Beowulf's introduction and Geatish identity from approximately line 194 onwards in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering), with Grendel's introduction, his descent from Cain, and his twelve-year depredations at Heorot narrated at lines 86–193 in the same line-numbering.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

Beowulf kills Grendel by tearing off his arm in a wrestling-match in the dark hall of Heorot, refusing weapons because Grendel uses none, and Grendel flees mortally wounded to die in the fenland-mere where his mother also dwells; the killing-encounter is at lines 710–836 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 710–836 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering, on the wrestling-match killing of Grendel, the arm-tearing, the weaponless contest, and Grendel's flight to the fen-mere.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The next night Grendel's mother attacks Heorot to avenge her son, and Beowulf descends into the underwater hall of the fen-mere to kill her with a giant-forged sword that he finds in the underwater hall itself; the encounter is at lines 1251–1887 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 1251–1887 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering, on Grendel's mother's revenge-attack, Beowulf's descent into the fen-mere, the underwater-hall combat, and the giant-forged sword.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The poem's third and final movement is set fifty years after the Grendel and Grendel's-mother encounters, with Beowulf now an old king of the Gēatas, when a treasure-hoarding dragon — the *draca* — that has been sleeping under an ancient burial-mound is roused by a thief who steals a single cup from the hoard and begins flying out by night to burn the Geatish countryside in revenge; the dragon-episode is at lines 2200–3182 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 2200–3182 in the standard Klaeber-edition line-numbering, on the fifty-year interval, the aged-king status, the thief-and-cup motif, the burial-mound location of the hoard, the dragon's nocturnal raids, and the third-movement framing; Watkins 1995 Part V on the dragon-slaying philological diction.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

Beowulf, now an old king, takes a small band of retainers to confront the dragon at the burial-mound; in the combat the dragon's fire is too much for his shield and his retainers flee except for the young Wiglaf, who stays with the king and helps him land the killing-blow on the dragon, but Beowulf is mortally wounded by the dragon's bite and dies in Wiglaf's arms after killing the dragon.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 2200–3182 (the dragon-episode), with the principal narrative beats at lines 2510–2820 (the combat itself, Wiglaf's loyalty, the killing of the dragon, and Beowulf's death-wound); Watkins 1995, Part V, on the IE-reflex reading of the dragon-killing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The aged-king status of Beowulf at the dragon-episode is the principal narrative-thematic distinction between the *Beowulf* dragon-combat and the standard heroic-initiation dragon-combat of the Sigurd-Fáfnir type: Beowulf has already done his major-hero deeds in the first two movements of the poem, and the dragon-combat is end-of-life and death-bringing rather than heroic-initiatory and life-establishing.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 2200–2820 on the aged-king status; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the contrast between aged-king dragon-combat and heroic-initiation dragon-combat patterns; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the contrast.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The aged-king-dies-killing-the-dragon pattern of *Beowulf* is also the principal thematic parallel to the Thor-Jörmungandr pattern of chapter 18 of the present volume: in both cases the hero kills the dragon and is mortally injured in the act, and both narratives end with the hero's death after the killing-stroke; the parallel is presented in the chapter as a comparative-typological observation rather than as evidence of direct textual connection.footnote:[*Beowulf*, lines 2200–3182 on the aged-king dragon-combat and the mutual-killing outcome; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the back-link to Ch. 18 (Thor-Jörmungandr) and on the aged-champion-dies-killing-the-serpent thematic rhyme; brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the Thor pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The *Beowulf* dragon is also distinct from Grendel and Grendel's mother at the level of morphology and conceptual category: Grendel and his mother are anthropoid-monstrous descendants-of-Cain whom the poem locates in a biblical-genealogical frame, while the dragon is a *draca* in the Old English category-sense and morphologically a serpentine fire-breathing hoard-guardian for whom the poem makes no biblical-genealogical commitment.footnote:[*Beowulf* on the descendants-of-Cain framing of Grendel (lines 102–114) and on the *draca* category (lines 2200ff); Watkins 1995 Part V on the *draca* as IE-formula reflex distinct from the biblical-Cain framing of Grendel.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

In Watkins's reading in *How to Kill a Dragon* Part V, the *Beowulf* dragon-episode preserves the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT poetic diction in the verbal phrasing of the killing and in the structural-narrative pattern of the combat, with the Old English dragon-vocabulary (*draca*, *wyrm*) and the killing-vocabulary instantiating the same IE-poetic structure that the Vedic, Hittite, and Norse comparanda instantiate in their respective languages.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the *Beowulf* dragon-episode as IE HERO SLAY SERPENT reflex in the killing-diction and the narrative-structure; on the Old English dragon-vocabulary (*draca*, *wyrm*) as IE-formula reflex.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

J. R. R. Tolkien's 1936 British Academy lecture "Beowulf: The Monsters and the Critics" argued that the dragon — and the monsters of the poem more generally — should be read as proper mythic adversaries integral to the poem's structure rather than as decorative accretions on a putatively-historical core, and the lecture was a load-bearing critical move that re-established the mythic register of the *Beowulf* monsters within twentieth-century Old English scholarship.footnote:[Tolkien, J. R. R., "Beowulf: The Monsters and the Critics", *Proceedings of the British Academy* 22 (1936): 245–295, on the dragon and the monsters of *Beowulf* as proper mythic adversaries; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the lecture's load-bearing-critical-move status for the chapter's Beowulf section and on the deferral of the lecture's literary-critical afterlife to the reception coda (Ch. 42).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=tolkien-1936-monsters ; loc=Proceedings of the British Academy 22 (1936)

The Tolkien 1936 lecture is mentioned in the present chapter only at the level of its load-bearing critical move (the recovery of the mythic register of the monsters), with the lecture's wider literary-critical afterlife — its influence on twentieth-century medievalism, on Tolkien's own dragon-fiction, and on the modern academic reading of the poem — explicitly deferred to the reception coda of the present book (Chapter 42), per the brief's special_instructions on the same deferral.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Tolkien-1936 mention as load-bearing critical move and on the deferral of the lecture's literary-critical afterlife to the reception coda Ch. 42; Tolkien 1936 (*Proc. British Academy* 22: 245–295); scope.md on the reception coda's chapter-placement.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=tolkien-1936-monsters ; loc=Proceedings of the British Academy 22 (1936)

The *Beowulf* dragon-episode's Indo-European-reflex reading therefore depends on two converging arguments: Watkins's philological argument that the dragon-killing diction reflects the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula, and Tolkien's structural-critical argument that the dragon is a proper mythic adversary integral to the poem; the chapter takes both arguments as load-bearing for the IE-reflex reading without committing to a wider claim about the poem's authorial-cultural position on Christian-vs-pagan inheritance.footnote:[Watkins 1995, Part V, on the philological argument; Tolkien 1936 (*Proc. British Academy* 22: 245–295) on the structural-critical argument; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the convergence of the two arguments and on the no-authorial-commitment guardrail.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=tolkien-1936-monsters ; loc=Proceedings of the British Academy 22 (1936)

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of the *Beowulf* poem as preserving an IE HERO SLAY SERPENT reflex in the dragon-episode, while consistent with Watkins 1995 Part V on the philological diction and with Tolkien 1936 on the structural-critical recovery of the mythic register of the monsters, is a comparative-philological reading rather than an unmediated feature of the poem itself; the *Beowulf*-poet's authorial-cultural position on the Christian-vs-pagan inheritance question is contested in modern scholarship and the chapter does not adjudicate the question, taking the IE-reflex reading as a poetic-formulaic observation rather than as a claim about the poet's own self-understanding. | basis: Watkins 1995 Part V on the IE-reflex philological reading; Tolkien 1936 on the mythic-register critical recovery; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the no-authorial-commitment guardrail | risk: the IE-reflex reading is comparative-philological and the chapter avoids claims about the *Beowulf*-poet's own authorial-cultural commitments.]

The principal forward-link from the *Beowulf* sub-section is to Part II C5 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula), in which the *Beowulf* dragon-killing diction joins the Vedic, Hittite, Norse, and Italic comparanda in the consolidated IE-formula argument; the principal back-link is to chapter 18 of the present volume (the Thor-Jörmungandr chapter), with the aged-champion-dies-killing-the-serpent thematic parallel as the load-bearing comparative observation.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the C5 forward-link and the Ch18 back-link; Watkins 1995, Part V, on the consolidated IE-formula argument; brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the Thor-pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

=== Lugh vs. Balor — the Second Battle of Mag Tuired

The Lugh-Balor narrative is preserved principally in the Middle Irish prose narrative *Cath Maige Tuired* ("The Battle of Mag Tuired"), transmitted in manuscripts from the 12th to the 16th century CE and presenting the Second Battle of Mag Tuired as the decisive combat between the Tuatha Dé Danann (the Irish gods-and-ancestors) and the Fomorians (a monstrous-and-oppressive people associated with the sea and with tyranny over the Tuatha Dé).footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired*, manuscripts 12th–16th c. CE; standard critical edition Gray 1982 (Irish Texts Society) — to be pinned at glossary-lock per inventory.approved.yaml identifier_missing flag; West 2007 ch. 6 on the Tuatha Dé Danann / Fomorians combat-axis; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Second Battle of Mag Tuired as the decisive Tuatha-Dé combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

Lugh is a young warrior-hero of the Tuatha Dé Danann, the son of Cian (a Tuatha Dé) and of Ethniu (the daughter of the Fomorian Balor), and his hybrid lineage — Tuatha Dé father and Fomorian mother — is the load-bearing genealogical motif of the *Cath Maige Tuired* narrative since it positions Lugh as the only figure who can kill his Fomorian grandfather Balor and end the Fomorian tyranny.footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired* on Lugh's hybrid lineage (Cian / Ethniu / Balor) and on the kill-the-grandfather motif; West 2007 ch. 6 on the same lineage as the IE-warrior structural-genealogical pattern; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the hybrid-lineage motif.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

Balor is the king (or principal warrior) of the Fomorians and is named in the *Cath Maige Tuired* as "Balor of the Evil Eye" (Balor Birug-derc, "Balor of the piercing eye") because of his single enormous destructive eye that can incinerate any host on which it is opened, and the tactical problem of the Second Battle of Mag Tuired is the problem of how to bring the eye down before it is opened on the Tuatha Dé host.footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired* on Balor's title "Balor of the Evil Eye" / Balor Birug-derc; on the destructive eye and the tactical-narrative problem it poses; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the evil-eye motif.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The killing-stroke at the Second Battle of Mag Tuired is delivered by Lugh with a sling-stone that strikes Balor's eye at the moment of the eye's opening, driving the eye through the back of Balor's head so that its destructive gaze is turned on the Fomorian host behind Balor and breaks their order; the *Cath Maige Tuired* narrates the killing-stroke as the decisive moment of the battle that ends the Fomorian tyranny and inaugurates Tuatha Dé Danann rule.footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired* on Lugh's sling-stone killing of Balor, on the eye-driven-through-the-head reversal, on the destruction of the Fomorian host by the redirected eye, and on the inauguration of Tuatha Dé rule; West 2007 ch. 6 on the same narrative-structural pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

[VARIANT: primary=*Cath Maige Tuired* prose narrative (Lugh kills Balor with a sling-stone; the principal-and-standard version) | alt=later folkloric variants in which the killing-weapon is a spear or *gae bolga* rather than a sling-stone (preserved in Irish-folkloric collections of the 19th and 20th centuries CE) | chosen: primary | reason: the sling-stone version is the version of the principal medieval prose narrative *Cath Maige Tuired*, and the spear-variants are later folkloric reworkings rather than independent medieval witnesses; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the sling-stone-as-standard-version with the spear-variant flagged.]

The *Cath Maige Tuired* narrative is structured around the wider battle-context of the Second Battle of Mag Tuired, in which the Tuatha Dé and the Fomorians fight a decisive engagement on the plain of Mag Tuired (in modern County Sligo), with the Lugh-Balor encounter as the decisive single-combat at the centre of the wider battle and with Lugh's status as battle-leader of the Tuatha Dé established by the prelude of the prose narrative.footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired* on the wider battle-context, on the plain of Mag Tuired (County Sligo) as the setting, and on Lugh's battle-leader role; West 2007 ch. 6 on the centred-single-combat structure of the IE warrior / dragon-slayer narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The First Battle of Mag Tuired is a separate earlier combat — narrated in the *Cath Maige Tuired Cunga* ("The First Battle of Mag Tuired") — in which the Tuatha Dé Danann defeat the Fir Bolg, and the *Cath Maige Tuired* of the present chapter is specifically the narrative of the Second Battle, with the Fomorian opponents and the Lugh-Balor encounter rather than the Fir Bolg opponents of the First Battle.footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired Cunga* (the First Battle of Mag Tuired) as a separate medieval narrative; *Cath Maige Tuired* of the present chapter as specifically the Second Battle narrative; West 2007 ch. 6 on the two-battle distinction in the medieval Irish corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The Lugh-Balor encounter is read by Indo-European comparativists — principally following Dumézil's twentieth-century treatments and West's 2007 synthesis — as a Celtic reflex of the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern, with Lugh in the warrior-pole and Balor in the morphologically-distinct adversary-pole; the Celtic reflex is morphologically distinctive in two respects, namely that Balor is not a serpentine adversary but a one-eyed giant, and that the killing-weapon is a projectile (sling-stone) rather than a sword or thunderbolt.footnote:[West 2007 ch. 6 on the Celtic reflex of the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern; on the morphologically-distinctive features of the Lugh-Balor encounter (one-eyed-giant adversary; sling-stone killing-weapon); brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the same morphological distinctiveness.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

[INFERENCE: the reading of the Lugh-Balor encounter as a Celtic reflex of the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern is a comparative-philological reading drawn from Dumézil's twentieth-century treatments and from West 2007 ch. 6, presented in the chapter as a comparative observation rather than as a feature of the *Cath Maige Tuired* itself; the medieval prose narrative does not in so many words frame the encounter as IE-comparative, and the IE-reflex reading is the chapter's editorial reading rather than the *Cath Maige Tuired*'s self-understanding. | basis: West 2007 ch. 6 on the IE warrior / dragon-slayer reading; Dumézil's twentieth-century treatments as the principal scholarly precedent for the IE-comparative reading; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the IE-reflex framing | risk: the *Cath Maige Tuired* itself frames the encounter within the medieval Irish narrative-tradition rather than within IE-comparative typology, and the chapter's IE-reflex reading is comparative rather than direct.]

Lugh's role in the *Cath Maige Tuired* narrative is also dynastic-political beyond the killing of Balor itself: the killing inaugurates Tuatha Dé Danann rule over Ireland in the medieval Irish pseudo-historical tradition, and Lugh becomes the principal Tuatha Dé warrior-king in the post-battle narrative.footnote:[*Cath Maige Tuired* on the post-battle Tuatha Dé Danann rule and on Lugh's warrior-king status; West 2007 ch. 6 on the dynastic-political function of the IE warrior / dragon-slayer narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The Lugh-Balor encounter's principal forward-link is to Part II C5 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula), in which the Celtic reflex joins the Vedic, Iranian, Anatolian, Norse, and Old English comparanda in the consolidated IE-formula argument, with the morphologically-distinctive features of the Celtic case (the one-eyed-giant adversary; the sling-stone killing-weapon) preserved in the comparative apparatus rather than smoothed away into a generic-warrior schema.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the C5 forward-link; West 2007 ch. 6 on the consolidated IE-comparative apparatus; on the preservation of morphologically-distinctive Celtic features in the comparative apparatus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

=== The Red Dragon and the White Dragon — Welsh combat

The Welsh Red-and-White Dragon narrative is preserved in two principal primary witnesses with distinct framings: the Latin *Historia Brittonum* (the Nennius-recension, conventionally dated c. 828 CE) gives a politicised version in which the two dragons are interpreted as the Britons and the Saxons, and the Middle Welsh prose tale *Lludd and Llefelys* in the *Mabinogion* (manuscripts from c. 1350 CE onward) preserves an older, less politicised version in which the combat is an annual cosmological-and-territorial disturbance to be ended by the king Lludd at the urging of his brother Llefelys.footnote:[*Historia Brittonum* c. 828 CE (Nennius recension) on the Britons-vs-Saxons political reading; *Lludd and Llefelys* in the *Mabinogion* (manuscripts White Book of Rhydderch c. 1350 CE; Red Book of Hergest c. 1382–1410 CE) on the annual-disturbance reading; Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the two-recension distinction; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the two-witness framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

In *Historia Brittonum* the combat-narrative is set within the Vortigern-and-Ambrosius episode: Vortigern, the British king, attempts to build a fortress at Dinas Emrys, but the foundations sink each night; Ambrosius (the boy-prophet who is, in the narrative, the figure who later becomes Merlin in the post-Geoffrey-of-Monmouth tradition) reveals that beneath the foundation-site is a hidden underground pool in which a red dragon and a white dragon fight, and the political-allegorical reading is that the red dragon is the Britons and the white dragon is the Saxons.footnote:[*Historia Brittonum* on the Vortigern-Ambrosius episode at Dinas Emrys, on the foundation-sinking, on the underground pool, on the red-dragon-vs-white-dragon combat, and on the Britons-vs-Saxons political-allegorical reading; Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the same.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

The political-allegorical reading of *Historia Brittonum* casts the Welsh Red-and-White Dragon combat as an early-medieval prophetic-and-political narrative — the red Welsh dragon will eventually defeat the white Saxon dragon, and the political-allegorical frame is load-bearing for the early-medieval recension of the narrative — and the red-dragon symbol enters the long-arc British (and specifically Welsh) political iconography from this *Historia Brittonum* moment onward.footnote:[*Historia Brittonum* on the political-allegorical frame and on the prophetic outcome; Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the long-arc Welsh-political iconographic reception of the red dragon.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

In *Lludd and Llefelys* the combat-narrative is one of three plagues that afflict the Island of Britain in the time of king Lludd: the first plague is the Coraniaid (a magical foreign people who hear all conversations), the second plague is the dragon-combat (an annual scream that destroys crops, terrifies pregnant women, and devastates the realm), and the third plague is a magical thief who steals the king's stores; Lludd, on his brother Llefelys's advice, addresses each of the three plagues in turn.footnote:[*Lludd and Llefelys* in the *Mabinogion* on the three-plague structure (Coraniaid; dragon-combat; magical thief); on Lludd's address to each plague on his brother Llefelys's advice; Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the three-plague structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

In *Lludd and Llefelys* the dragon-combat is presented as an annual cosmological-and-territorial disturbance: every May Eve the two dragons rise from beneath the centre of Britain and fight in the air with a scream that shakes the realm and destroys crops, and the resolution prescribed by Llefelys is to find the centre of the Island, dig a pit, fill the pit with mead, lure the two dragons into the pit, capture them when they are drunk on the mead, and bury them in a stone chest at Dinas Emrys.footnote:[*Lludd and Llefelys* on the May-Eve annual disturbance, on the central location of the combat, on the mead-pit-and-burial resolution, and on the Dinas Emrys burial-site; Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the resolution-mechanism.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

[VARIANT: primary=*Historia Brittonum* (red-and-white-dragon political allegory; Britons-vs-Saxons reading; the dragons fight beneath Dinas Emrys and Vortigern's fortress sinks; the prophetic-political resolution) | alt=*Lludd and Llefelys* in the *Mabinogion* (annual May-Eve disturbance; the dragons are buried at Dinas Emrys after being lured into a mead-pit; cosmological-territorial resolution rather than political-allegorical) | chosen: present-both | reason: the two recensions present two distinct framings of the same underlying combat-tradition, with *Historia Brittonum* the political-allegorical reading of c. 828 CE and *Lludd and Llefelys* the older non-political reading of the *Mabinogion* tradition; per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the two-witness side-by-side presentation.]

The annual-disturbance reading of *Lludd and Llefelys* preserves an older, non-political layer of the dragon-tradition in which the combat is a cyclical-cosmological disturbance to be ended rather than a political-prophetic conflict to be resolved in favour of one party, and the *Lludd and Llefelys* narrative is treated by Welsh-philological scholarship as a witness to the older substrate of the dragon-tradition that the *Historia Brittonum* recension has subsequently politicised.footnote:[*Lludd and Llefelys* on the cyclical-cosmological framing; Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the older-substrate-vs-politicised-recension distinction; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the older-substrate reading of *Lludd and Llefelys*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

The Dinas Emrys site — a hilltop in Snowdonia in present-day north Wales — is the principal geographical anchor of both the *Historia Brittonum* and the *Lludd and Llefelys* recensions, and the archaeological evidence of pre-Roman and post-Roman occupation at the hilltop has been read by some Welsh archaeologists as a possible cultic locus to which the dragon-tradition was already attached at the time of the early-medieval recension; the chapter cites the archaeological reading at corpus level rather than as a specific archaeological-finding claim.footnote:[Dinas Emrys (Snowdonia, north Wales) as the geographical anchor of both *Historia Brittonum* and *Lludd and Llefelys*; Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the site and on the archaeological-cultic reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

[INFERENCE: the reading of *Lludd and Llefelys* as preserving an older, non-politicised layer of the Welsh dragon-tradition than the *Historia Brittonum* political allegory, while consistent with Ogden 2013 on the older-substrate-vs-politicised-recension distinction and with the brief's 19-ie-reflexes.yaml framing, is a comparative-philological reading rather than an unmediated chronological claim about the two recensions; the *Mabinogion* manuscripts are later in transmission-date than *Historia Brittonum* but the reading takes the *Lludd and Llefelys* material as preserving older oral-tradition substrate. | basis: Ogden 2013 on the older-substrate reading; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the substrate-vs-politicisation distinction; *Lludd and Llefelys* on the cyclical-cosmological framing | risk: the substrate-vs-politicisation reading is a philological-historical reading rather than a direct manuscript-dated chronology, and the *Mabinogion* manuscripts are later than the *Historia Brittonum* recension.]

The Welsh Red-and-White Dragon narrative is structurally distinctive within the IE-reflex corpus: the combat is between two dragons rather than between a hero and a dragon, with the human-political resolution (Vortigern's fortress; Lludd's mead-pit) supplied as an external resolution to a combat that is otherwise dragon-on-dragon, and the narrative therefore stands at the edge of the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT pattern rather than as a central instance.footnote:[*Historia Brittonum* and *Lludd and Llefelys* on the dragon-on-dragon combat-structure; Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the structural-distinctiveness reading; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the edge-of-pattern status of the Welsh case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

The Welsh Red-and-White Dragon narrative's principal forward-link is to Part II C5 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula), in which the Welsh case enters as a Celtic instance whose structural distinctiveness is preserved in the comparative apparatus, and to Part II C8 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the appropriation problem) in respect of the political-allegorical reading of *Historia Brittonum* as an early-medieval Christian-historiographical reframing.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the C5 forward-link and the political-allegorical reading; Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the comparative apparatus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

The Welsh Red-and-White Dragon narrative is also iconographically durable in the long-arc Welsh political tradition: the red dragon is preserved as an emblem of Wales in medieval and modern Welsh political symbolism, including the modern Welsh national flag, and the iconographic durability is the principal cultural-relevance datum for the Welsh sub-section's reception-history dimension.footnote:[Ogden 2013 ch. on Celtic material on the long-arc iconographic durability of the red dragon as emblem of Wales; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml cultural_relevance entry on the national-emblem function of the Welsh dragon.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ogden-2013 ; loc=ch. on Celtic material

=== Batraz and the Nart combats — Ossetic

The Batraz narrative is preserved in the Ossetic Nart saga corpus, an oral-epic tradition recorded in the 19th and 20th centuries CE from Ossetian, Circassian, and Chechen narrators across the North Caucasus, with the principal scholarly anchor Georges Dumézil's twentieth-century treatments and the principal IE-comparative apparatus West 2007's chapter 6.footnote:[Ossetic Nart saga corpus, 19th–20th c. CE oral-epic recordings from Ossetian, Circassian, and Chechen narrators; Dumézil's twentieth-century treatments as the principal scholarly precedent for the IE-comparative reading; West 2007 ch. 6 on the Scytho-Alanic IE warrior / dragon-slayer reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The Narts are the heroic figures of the North Caucasian epic tradition — a category-class of warrior-figures who occupy the epic narrative-space and who are the principal characters of the Ossetic Nart saga corpus and of the cognate Circassian and Chechen Nart traditions — with Batraz one of the principal Nart warrior-figures in the Ossetic version of the corpus.footnote:[Ossetic Nart saga corpus on the Narts as heroic figures of the North Caucasian epic tradition; West 2007 ch. 6 on Batraz as a principal Ossetic Nart warrior-figure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

Batraz is distinguished within the Ossetic Nart corpus by his steel body — he is born from a tumour or boil on the back of his father Khamits, plunges himself into a furnace, and emerges with a body of steel — and the steel-body motif is the principal physical-and-narrative attribute that distinguishes Batraz from other Narts within the corpus.footnote:[Ossetic Nart saga corpus on Batraz's steel-body origin (born from Khamits's back; furnace-tempering; steel-body status); West 2007 ch. 6 on the steel-body motif as IE-warrior structural attribute.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

Batraz fights monstrous giants and dragons across multiple episodes of the Nart corpus: against the giant Mukara, against various dragons-and-monstrous-figures, and against opposing Nart-clans within the Ossetic tradition; the Nart corpus is not a single continuous narrative but a cluster of episodic combats, with each combat-episode preserving the warrior-pole of the IE pattern in distinctive Ossetic morphology.footnote:[Ossetic Nart saga corpus on Batraz's combats against the giant Mukara and against various other giant and dragon adversaries within the corpus; West 2007 ch. 6 on the episodic-cluster structure of the Nart corpus and on the warrior-pole preservation; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the episodic-cluster structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

In Dumézil's twentieth-century reading, Batraz is the Scytho-Alanic Indo-European reflex of the IE warrior / dragon-slayer pattern — preserved within the Ossetic-and-cognate Nart traditions as the indigenous-and-uninterrupted continuation of an ancient Iranian-Indo-European warrior-figure type — and the steel-body motif is read by Dumézil as a distinctively-developed feature of the warrior-pole within the Scytho-Alanic stratum.footnote:[West 2007 ch. 6 on Dumézil's reading of Batraz as Scytho-Alanic IE warrior / dragon-slayer reflex; on the Ossetic-and-cognate Nart traditions as the uninterrupted Scytho-Alanic continuation; on the steel-body motif as Scytho-Alanic distinctive development.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The Ossetian, Circassian, and Chechen Nart corpora preserve multiple recorded variants of the Batraz combats across narrators, with the Ossetic-narrator variants forming the principal corpus for the Dumézilian IE-comparative reading and the Circassian-and-Chechen variants providing comparative-and-control material whose narrative-detail differences are the subject of twentieth- and twenty-first-century North Caucasian folkloric and philological scholarship.footnote:[Ossetic, Circassian, and Chechen Nart corpora on the multiple-narrator variant structure; West 2007 ch. 6 on the variant-comparative methodological framing; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the multiple-narrator-variant structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

Batraz's death in the Ossetic Nart corpus is itself a thematic motif within the warrior-and-combat cycle: in the principal Ossetic-variant version of his death-narrative he is killed when the gods cast him from the heavens into a body of water, his steel body cooled-and-shattered against the resistance of the water, in a death-by-water that some twentieth-century Ossetic-folkloric readings interpret as cognate with other IE warrior-death narratives.footnote:[Ossetic Nart saga corpus on Batraz's death-narrative (cast-from-heavens; cooled-and-shattered against water); West 2007 ch. 6 on the IE-comparative reading of the death-narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

[INFERENCE: the reading of Batraz as a Scytho-Alanic IE warrior / dragon-slayer reflex, while consistent with Dumézil's twentieth-century treatments and with West 2007 ch. 6, is a comparative-philological reading drawn from Indo-European-comparativist scholarship rather than an unmediated feature of the Ossetic Nart corpus itself; the Nart-narrators do not in so many words frame Batraz as IE-comparative, and twentieth-century Caucasian-area-specialist scholarship has at times raised methodological reservations about the directness of the Dumézilian IE-comparative reading. | basis: West 2007 ch. 6 on the IE-reflex reading; Dumézil's twentieth-century treatments as the principal precedent; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the IE-reflex framing | risk: the IE-reflex reading is comparative-philological, the Nart-narrators frame Batraz within the indigenous Caucasian epic tradition, and Caucasian-area-specialist scholarship retains methodological reservations on the directness of the Dumézilian reading.]

The Batraz sub-case is centrally located in the Ossetic tradition and marginally located outside it: within the Ossetic-and-cognate Nart corpora Batraz is a principal warrior-figure in a load-bearing epic tradition, and outside the North Caucasian context the narrative is known principally through Dumézil's twentieth-century IE-comparative monographs and through the more general handbooks of Indo-European mythology, with limited direct circulation outside the comparativist scholarly literature.footnote:[West 2007 ch. 6 on the central-Ossetic / marginal-outside reception-pattern of the Batraz material; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml cultural_relevance entry on Batraz as central in Ossetic tradition and marginal outside.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The Batraz sub-case's principal forward-link is to Part II C5 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula), in which the Scytho-Alanic reflex enters as a Caucasian-Iranian instance whose Ossetic-Nart preservation supplies the eastern-comparative material to set against the Western Indo-European reflexes (Norse, Old English, Celtic) of the present chapter's other sub-cases.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the C5 forward-link; West 2007 ch. 6 on the Scytho-Alanic / Caucasian-Iranian comparative material; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml cross_cultural_parallels entry on the C5-feeder status of the Batraz sub-case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

=== Vahagn and the vishaps — Armenian (fragmentary)

The Vahagn material is the most fragmentary of the six sub-cases of the present chapter, and the Vahagn sub-section is presented with explicit fragmentary framing throughout: the principal hymn-text to Vahagn is lost, and what survives is only Movses Khorenatsi's late paraphrase of an earlier Armenian hymn in book I chapter 31 of the *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* ("History of the Armenians").footnote:[Khorenatsi *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* book I.31 on the Vahagn paraphrase; Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the lost-hymn-and-late-paraphrase status of the Vahagn material; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the explicit fragmentary framing required for the Vahagn sub-section.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

[LACUNA: the principal hymn-text to Vahagn is lost; what survives is only Khorenatsi's late paraphrase of the hymn in book I chapter 31 of the *Patmut'iwn Hayots'*. | source: Khorenatsi *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* I.31 (preserving the late paraphrase); the hymn-text itself is not extant in any manuscript witness; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the lost-hymn status | scholarly_reconstruction: Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter reads Khorenatsi's paraphrase as preserving the substantive content of the lost hymn at the level of the dragon-slayer-epithet (Vishapakagh, "dragon-reaper") and the storm-god combat-frame, with the specific verbal-poetic diction of the lost hymn unrecoverable; Petrosyan's Armenian-mythology scholarship corroborates the Russell reading at the level of substantive content while flagging the verbal-philological limits of the reconstruction.]

Vahagn is the Armenian storm-god whose epithet *Vishapakagh* — "dragon-reaper" — preserves in nominal form the dragon-slayer combat-pattern and identifies him as Armenia's storm-god dragon-slayer in the same conceptual category-position that Indra occupies in the Vedic tradition, Thraētaona in the Iranian tradition, and Thor in the Norse tradition.footnote:[Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on Vahagn as the Armenian storm-god and on the *Vishapakagh* "dragon-reaper" epithet; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the storm-god dragon-slayer category-position.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

Khorenatsi's *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* — conventionally dated to the 5th to 9th century CE in successive scholarly assessments, with the precise dating contested in twentieth-century Armenian scholarship — is itself a relatively-late and historiographical witness to the pre-Christian Armenian religious culture, and Khorenatsi's paraphrase of the Vahagn hymn at I.31 is presented within his work as a document of Armenian pagan-religious tradition on which his own Christian-historiographical narrative depends for its pre-Christian background.footnote:[Khorenatsi *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* on the historiographical-and-Christian framing of the work; Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the dating-contested status of Khorenatsi's composition (5th–9th c. CE bracketing) and on the historiographical-paraphrase status of the Vahagn passage at I.31.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

Khorenatsi's paraphrase at *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* I.31 describes Vahagn's birth from the heavens, the sea, and a reed — a triple-birth motif in which the heavens labour, the sea labours, the purple reed labours, and Vahagn emerges from the smoke of the reed with sun-like hair and beard and fire-like eyes — and the paraphrase preserves this birth-narrative without supplying a continuous narrative of Vahagn's combat with the *vishaps* themselves.footnote:[Khorenatsi *Patmut'iwn Hayots'* I.31 on the triple-birth motif (heavens / sea / reed; sun-like hair; fire-like eyes); Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the birth-narrative as preserved in the paraphrase and on the absence of continuous combat-narrative; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the paraphrase contents.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

The Vahagn combat-with-the-*vishaps* is therefore not preserved as a continuous narrative in any extant primary witness, and the chapter's combat-content for the Vahagn sub-case is reconstructed from the *Vishapakagh* dragon-reaper epithet, from the storm-god category-position of Vahagn within the Armenian pantheon, from the *vishapakar* stelae of the second millennium BCE (which document the cultic-and-iconographic context of the dragon-figure within Armenian pre-Christian religion), and from comparative-philological readings of the surviving paraphrase against the Vedic, Iranian, and Anatolian comparanda.footnote:[Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the reconstruction-from-epithet, from-category-position, from-cultic-stelae, and from-comparative-philology methodology for the Vahagn combat-content; *vishapakar* stelae as the cultic-iconographic-context evidence; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the reconstruction methodology and on the explicit fragmentary framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

The *vishaps* are the dragons of the Armenian tradition — the term *vishap* is the Armenian common noun for "dragon", attested both as a narrative-mythological category and as the name given to the second-millennium-BCE stone stelae (the *vishapakar* or "dragon-stones") that survive in the highlands of historical Armenia — and the *vishapakar* stelae are the principal pre-Christian-period material attestation of the dragon-figure within the Armenian cultic-archaeological context.footnote:[Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the *vishap* as Armenian common noun for "dragon" and on the *vishapakar* stelae of the second millennium BCE as the principal pre-Christian-period material attestation; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the *vishapakar* stelae as cultic-iconographic-context evidence.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

The *vishapakar* stelae are dated by Armenian archaeologists to the second millennium BCE and are concentrated in the high pastures of the Armenian highlands (principally on the slopes of Mount Aragats and in the Geghama mountains), with the stelae's morphology featuring fish-like or serpentine-like carvings on tall stone shafts; the cultic-iconographic context of the stelae is inferred from their location and morphology, and the Armenian-archaeological literature reads the stelae as cultic objects associated with water, fertility, and dragon-figures.footnote:[Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the second-millennium-BCE dating, on the Aragats / Geghama-mountains location, on the fish-and-serpentine morphology, and on the cultic-iconographic reading of the *vishapakar* stelae; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the *vishapakar* archaeological context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

[INFERENCE: the reading of Vahagn as the Armenian Indo-European reflex of HERO SLAY SERPENT — given the lost-hymn status of the principal narrative source and the reconstruction of the combat-content from epithet, category-position, *vishapakar* archaeological context, and comparative-philological readings — is a heavily-comparative reading whose evidential basis is fragmentary in a way that none of the other five sub-cases of the present chapter is; the chapter presents the Vahagn reading with explicit fragmentary framing and refrains from reconstructing combat-content beyond what Khorenatsi's paraphrase, the *Vishapakagh* epithet, and the *vishapakar* stelae jointly support. | basis: Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the four-source reconstruction methodology (paraphrase / epithet / stelae / comparative); Petrosyan's Armenian-mythology scholarship on the same; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the explicit-fragmentary-framing requirement | risk: the reading depends on a much-thinner evidential base than the Norse, Old English, or Celtic sub-cases, and the chapter's presentation of the Vahagn material is correspondingly more circumspect about combat-content claims.]

In Russell's reading in the Vahagn chapter of *Zoroastrianism in Armenia*, Vahagn is integrated into the wider Indo-Iranian and Indo-European combat-tradition as the Armenian preservation of an inherited storm-god dragon-slayer figure, with the Iranian *Vərəθraγna* (the Avestan-and-Pahlavi smashing-of-resistance figure) and the Vedic Indra-Vṛtra as the principal Indo-Iranian comparanda and with the *Vishapakagh* epithet as the load-bearing philological exhibit for the inheritance.footnote:[Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the Indo-Iranian and Indo-European integration of Vahagn; on the *Vərəθraγna* (Iranian) and Indra-Vṛtra (Vedic) comparanda; on the *Vishapakagh* epithet as the load-bearing philological exhibit; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Indo-Iranian-comparative reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

Petrosyan's Armenian-mythology scholarship corroborates Russell's reading at the level of substantive content while flagging the verbal-philological limits of the reconstruction: the *Vishapakagh* epithet, the *vishapakar* archaeological context, and the storm-god category-position are read by Petrosyan as joint evidence for the dragon-slayer combat-pattern, but the verbal-poetic diction of the lost hymn is not recoverable and the chapter retains this limit in its presentation of the Vahagn material.footnote:[Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the convergence with Petrosyan's Armenian-mythology scholarship; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the verbal-philological limits of the Vahagn reconstruction; on the chapter's retention of these limits in presentation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

The Vahagn sub-case's principal forward-link is to Part II C5 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula), in which the Armenian reflex enters with explicit fragmentary framing as the most-fragmentary instance of the IE pattern within the chapter's six sub-cases, and the C5 chapter is therefore the locus where the chapter's heaviest comparative-reconstruction-marker pattern is presented in consolidated form.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the C5 forward-link with explicit fragmentary framing; Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the comparative-IE position of the Armenian material; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml cross_cultural_parallels entry on the C5-feeder status.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=russell-zoroastrianism-armenia ; loc=Vahagn chapter

=== Family pattern, reception, and forward links

The six sub-cases treated in the chapter — Sigurd-Fáfnir, Beowulf-Grendel-and-the-dragon, Lugh-Balor, the Welsh Red-and-White Dragon, Batraz, and Vahagn-and-the-*vishaps* — are arranged on a philological-evidence spectrum from the strongest evidence (Sigurd, where Watkins reads the Eddic diction as a direct IE-formula reflex in chapter 42 of the volume) to the most fragmentary (Vahagn, where the principal hymn-text is lost and the combat-content is reconstructed from the *Vishapakagh* epithet, Khorenatsi's late paraphrase, and the *vishapakar* archaeological context).footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the philological-evidence-spectrum ordering; Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on the Sigurd-strongest evidence; Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the Vahagn-most-fragmentary evidence.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

The chapter's six sub-cases vary in IE-philological-evidence weight along three dimensions: the existence and accessibility of the principal primary text (extant Eddic poems for Sigurd; single-manuscript Old English epic for Beowulf; medieval Irish prose for Lugh-Balor; two co-equal medieval recensions for the Welsh dragons; oral-epic recordings for Batraz; lost hymn and late paraphrase for Vahagn), the directness of the philological-formulaic reading (Watkins's direct-formula reading for Sigurd and *Beowulf*; structural-typological reading for Lugh-Balor and Welsh dragons; Dumézilian comparative reading for Batraz; epithet-and-archaeological reading for Vahagn), and the centrality of the figure within its tradition (central in Old Norse and Old English; significant in Irish and Welsh; central in Ossetic and marginal outside; fragmentary in Armenian).footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the three-dimension framing of the philological-evidence-spectrum; Watkins 1995 Part V on the philological-formulaic reading; West 2007 ch. 6 on the structural-typological reading; Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter on the Armenian-fragmentary status.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The chapter's overall argument is therefore that the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT pattern is preserved across these six post-Vedic, post-Iranian, post-Anatolian Indo-European traditions in different evidential modalities — Old Norse Eddic poetic-formulaic; Old English epic-narrative-and-formulaic; Middle Irish prose-and-narrative; Welsh prose-recension-pair; Ossetic oral-epic-and-IE-comparative; Armenian fragmentary-paraphrase-and-archaeological — and that the family-chapter format is justified by the shared inheritance of the pattern across the divergent evidential modalities rather than by a uniform-evidential-mode reading.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the family-chapter justification by shared inheritance across divergent evidential modalities; Watkins 1995 Part V on the consolidated IE-pattern argument; West 2007 ch. 6 on the storm-god / water-dragon complex across the Indo-European traditions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The chapter's principal forward-link is to Part II C5 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula), in which the six sub-cases of the present chapter join the Vedic Indra-Vṛtra material (Ch14), the Avestan Aži Dahāka material (Ch13), the Hittite Illuyanka material (Ch6), the Norse Thor-Jörmungandr material (Ch18), and the Greek Geryon and Italic Cacus material (Ch12) in the consolidated IE-formula argument; the chapter is the main feeder to that comparative chapter.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the chapter as the main feeder to Part II C5; Watkins 1995 Part V on the consolidated IE-formula argument; brief 12-greek-dragons-after-typhon.yaml on the Geryon and Cacus material; brief 14-indra-vrtra.yaml and brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the Vedic and Norse material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The chapter's principal back-link is to chapter 18 of the present volume (the Thor-Jörmungandr chapter), with two specific connections: the Sigurd-Fáfnir Eddic-iconographic-philological triple-witness pattern parallels the Thor-Jörmungandr triple-witness pattern across the same Old Norse evidence-base, and the *Beowulf* aged-king-dies-killing-the-dragon thematic pattern parallels the Thor-Ragnarök mutual-killing thematic pattern at the level of the heroic-death-after-killing structure.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Ch18 back-link with Sigurd-Norse-parallel and Beowulf-aged-champion-parallel components; Larrington 2014 on the Sigurd-Eddic-iconographic dossier; brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the Thor-Jörmungandr triple-witness pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd-cycle

The chapter's secondary forward-link is to chapter 42 of the present book (the reception coda), in which the literary-critical afterlife of Tolkien's 1936 *Beowulf* essay is treated alongside the post-medieval reception of the Sigurd-cycle in Wagner and modern dragon-fiction, with the present chapter restricting itself to the load-bearing-critical-move dimension of Tolkien 1936 and deferring the wider literary-critical afterlife to Ch42 per the brief's special_instructions.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the deferral of the Tolkien-essay afterlife to the reception coda Ch42; scope.md on the reception-coda chapter-placement; on the present chapter's restriction to the load-bearing-critical-move dimension.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The chapter's tertiary forward-link is to Part II C7 of the present book (the comparative chapter on the inversion problem), in which Sigurd's ambiguous outcome — the killing brings the hero the treasure but also the curse that will destroy him — joins the Bida case (Ch29) as a load-bearing exhibit for the inversion problem within the IE-tradition itself, with the present chapter establishing the Sigurd-cycle's curse-driven trajectory as the basis for the C7 development.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Sigurd-cycle / C7 forward-link; Larrington 2014 on the curse-driven trajectory of the Sigurd-cycle; on the C7 inversion-problem development.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd-cycle long arc

The chapter's quaternary forward-link is to chapter 20 of the present book (the Slavic-Baltic family chapter on the Perun-Veles "basic myth" reconstruction and the Dobrynya byliny), with which the present chapter shares IE-formula context: the Slavic-Baltic case extends the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT pattern further into the post-Bronze-Age and post-Antique Indo-European traditions, and the two chapters together cover the wider IE-reflex apparatus that feeds Part II C5.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the Ch20 forward-link with shared IE-formula context; on the Slavic-Baltic chapter as next IE-reflex chapter in the book's Part I sequence; West 2007 ch. 6 on the Slavic-Baltic Perun-Veles material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=west-2007 ; loc=ch. 6

The chapter's joint contribution to the present book is therefore in three parts: the chapter supplies the principal post-Vedic, post-Iranian, post-Anatolian IE-reflex apparatus for Part II C5; the chapter establishes the inversion-foreshadowing reading of the Sigurd-cycle for Part II C7; and the chapter situates the Tolkien 1936 *Beowulf* essay's load-bearing critical move within the Beowulf sub-section while deferring the essay's literary-critical afterlife to Ch42, the reception coda.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the three-part contribution; Watkins 1995 Part V on the C5-feeder status; brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the C7-inversion forward-link; on the Tolkien-1936 / Ch42 deferral.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

The chapter's overall position within the Indo-European cluster of the present book is as the family-chapter that consolidates the post-Vedic, post-Iranian, post-Anatolian IE-reflexes that the flagship chapters (Ch13 Aži Dahāka; Ch14 Indra-Vṛtra; Ch18 Thor-Jörmungandr) treat individually, with the chapter's six sub-cases supplying the comparative breadth that the flagship chapters do not — by design — supply on their own; the chapter is therefore the principal IE-reflex consolidation in Part I of the book.footnote:[Per brief 19-ie-reflexes.yaml on the consolidation function of the chapter within the Part I IE-cluster; Watkins 1995 Part V on the IE-reflex apparatus; brief 13-azidahaka.yaml; brief 14-indra-vrtra.yaml; brief 18-thor-jormungandr.yaml on the flagship chapters.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (Watkins 1995 ch. 42 for Sigurd; Part V for Beowulf, Lugh, Welsh, Batraz, Vahagn — Part II C5 main feeder)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Norse (Thor-Jörmungandr triple-witness pattern paralleled by Sigurd-Fáfnir Eddic-iconographic dossier; aged-champion-dies-killing-the-serpent pattern parallel between Beowulf and Thor — Ch18 back-link)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Inversion problem (Sigurd's ambiguous outcome — the killing brings the curse that destroys the hero — paired with Bida as IE-internal inversion exhibit, Part II C7)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Reception coda (Tolkien 1936 *Beowulf* essay as load-bearing critical move for the chapter's Beowulf section; the essay's literary-critical afterlife deferred to Ch42)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Slavic-Baltic IE-reflex (Perun-Veles "basic myth" reconstruction and Dobrynya byliny — Ch20; shared IE-formula context as next IE-reflex chapter in Part I)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Indo-Iranian comparanda (Vahagn / Vərəθraγna / Indra-Vṛtra storm-god dragon-slayer category-position — Russell 1987 Vahagn chapter; back-link to Ch13 Aži Dahāka and Ch14 Indra-Vṛtra)
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Cyclical-cosmological annual disturbance (*Lludd and Llefelys* annual May-Eve dragon-combat as preserved older substrate — comparative parallel to Hittite Illuyanka *purulli* festival, Ch6, and to Apollo-Python Septerion festival, Ch12)

# Input file: chapters/15-vedic-reflexes.adoc

[[ch-15-vedic-reflexes]]
== Vedic and Post-Vedic Reflexes (Trita Āptya, Krishna/Kāliya)

=== Source overview

This chapter takes up two clearly-distinct Indian dragon-combat sub-cases that bracket the Indra-Vṛtra flagship of the preceding chapter: the older Indo-Iranian Trita Āptya stratum, attested in a small cluster of Rigvedic hymns, and the bhakti-era Krishna-Kāliya reworking, attested in the *Harivaṃśa*, the *Viṣṇu Purāṇa*, and the *Bhāgavata Purāṇa*.footnote:[Per brief 15-vedic-reflexes.yaml; Jamison and Brereton 2014 on the Trita Āptya material in the Rigvedic corpus; Forsyth 1987, ch. 2, on the long arc of Indian dragon-combat material from the Rigveda to the bhakti-period reworkings.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=Trita Āptya material

The chapter's primary text for the Trita Āptya material is the Rigveda (Sanskrit Ṛgveda), the same Vedic Sanskrit corpus that supplies the Indra-Vṛtra material of the preceding chapter, cited here at hymn-and-verse granularity (RV book.hymn.verse) keyed to Stephanie Jamison and Joel Brereton's three-volume *The Rigveda: The Earliest Religious Poetry of India*, published by Oxford University Press in 2014.footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, *The Rigveda: The Earliest Religious Poetry of India*, three-volume Oxford translation, ISBN 9780199370184, used as the working English text for all Rigvedic material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=publisher and edition

The Trita Āptya material is distributed across the Rigvedic corpus in hymn-allusive rather than narrative-continuous form, and the chapter's working textual base for the figure is the small cluster of hymns in which Trita is named as a combat-figure: principally RV 10.99.6 (the standard locus for Trita's slaying of a three-headed adversary) and RV 1.105 and RV 2.11.19–20, in which Trita appears alongside Indra in the combat-context.footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, on the Trita Āptya material in RV 10.99.6, 1.105, and 2.11.19–20; brief 15-vedic-reflexes.yaml, sources.primary, listing the Trita-cluster hymns.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=RV 10.99.6; 1.105; 2.11.19–20

Jamison and Brereton's translation is printed-only in the present project's working corpus, and the chapter cites it at hymn-and-verse level for specific Trita passages and at section level for general points about the corpus, in line with the citation discipline applied throughout the Iran-and-India cluster of the present book.footnote:[Jamison and Brereton 2014, three-volume printed edition; cited at hymn-and-verse level for specific RV passages and at section level for corpus-wide points, per the established discipline of chapters 13 and 14.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=jamison-brereton-2014 ; loc=printed-only; cited at hymn-and-verse level

The chapter's foundational Indo-Iranian comparative anchor for the Trita Āptya / Thraētaona cognancy is Émile Benveniste and Louis Renou's *Vṛtra et Vṛθragna: Étude de mythologie indo-iranienne*, published in 1934 in the Cahiers de la Société Asiatique series and available digitally in scanned form via Gallica BnF.footnote:[Benveniste and Renou 1934, *Vṛtra et Vṛθragna: Étude de mythologie indo-iranienne*, Cahiers de la Société Asiatique 3 (Paris: Imprimerie Nationale, 1934); stable URL gallica.bnf.fr.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=benveniste-renou-1934 ; loc=monograph as a whole
